Suspected bombs exploded outside the U.S. embassies in the capitals of Kenya and Tanzania on Friday, killing dozens of people, witnesses said.
The American ambassador to Kenya was among hundreds injured, local television reported.
"It was definitely a bomb," said a U.S. Embassy official in Nairobi who did not want to be identified.
"You can see a huge crater behind the building and at the same time a bomb exploded in the embassy in Tanzania," he said.
Terrorist attacks on US embassies in Kenya and Tanzania blew up buildings and buses, trapping people under concrete slabs and twisted steel that rescuers cleared with cranes, flashlights and bare hands.
At least 82 people were killed and more than 1,700 injured, authorities said at dawn on Saturday.
Dozens of people are still missing and the death toll is expected to rise, it said.
The suspected car bomb attacks occurred 700 kilometres apart on Friday, but only minutes apart, turning busy streets in two African capitals into bloody piles of concrete and steel knots.
"This appears to have been a very well-coordinated, very well-planned attack - clearly not the work of amateurs," U.S. National Security Council spokesman P.J. Crowley said in Washington.
No one has claimed responsibility.

"We extend our deepest condolences to the victims of these atrocities and their families in Kenya, Tanzania and the United States," Estrada said in a statement.
"No cause is so great as to justify wanton murder and mutilation of innocents."
In total, bombings in Kenya and Tanzania last week killed at least 217 people and injured nearly 5,000.
The Security Council on Thursday condemned the bombing of the US embassies in Kenya and Tanzania and called on all states to cooperate with authorities in the search for those responsible.
A resolution supported and unanimously adopted by Kenya, Tanzania and the United States "expresses its deep sorrow, sympathy and condolences to the families of the innocent victims of the terrorist attacks at this difficult time."
It condemns the "terrorist bombings in the strongest possible terms."
And it stresses that every nation "has a duty not to organize, incite, support, or participate in terrorist attacks in another state."

US Secretary of State Madeleine Albright paid tribute to the bombing victims and embassy staff during a lightning visit to East Africa on Tuesday, promising to track down the perpetrators.
"Together we promise to bring the killers of our friends to justice," she told American and Tanzanian workers at the bombed-out U.S. Embassy in Dar es Salaam, Tanzania.
Officials in Tanzania and Kenya, where she arrived next, also hoped she would provide new aid to their countries, where the August 7 terrorist attacks killed 257 people in Nairobi and Dar es Salaam.
FBI agents, meanwhile, pressed ahead with their extensive investigation into the bombings.
Contrary to other reports, the FBI and Kenyan police said Monday that a suspect who returned from Pakistan after three days of questioning did not admit any role in the crimes or implicate others.
Albright, who arrived after a 16-hour flight, spent about five hours in Tanzania before flying to Nairobi on a small plane when an engine on the Boeing 747 flying in from Washington overheated.
She was due to lay flowers at the bombed-out embassy, speak to Kenyan embassy guards and other survivors of the blast and meet President Daniel arap Moi in a five-hour visit before returning home.
In Dar es Salaam, she laid a wreath next to the crater left by the embassy explosion that killed 10 people.
She then handed the US Marines a folded American flag and, wearing a white construction helmet, inspected the damage inside.
Reporters were also allowed inside for the first time since the explosion and found shards of glass and smashed furniture scattered around the embassy.
Albright helped put up a poster offering a reward of up to $2 million for information leading to the arrest of the assailants.
She then showered embassy staff with praise and the State Department's award for heroism before meeting bomb victims at Muhimbili Medical Center and government officials.
"America is very proud of you, all of you, Americans and Tanzanians," she told the staff.
Albright announced a donation of 500 pounds (225 kilograms) of medical supplies to Tanzania and Kenya from Walter Reed Army Medical Center.
She also promised to ask Congress to authorize an unspecified amount of emergency aid to fund the resumption of embassy operations in Tanzania and Kenya and to compensate the families of the victims.
Officials from the two African countries expect significant compensation and aid pledges for governments whose key tourism industries have been hit by the bombings.

Poland, Hungary and the Czech Republic can now participate in NATO's strategic planning because all NATO member states have agreed to join, German Defense Minister Rudolf Scharping said on Saturday.
Earlier this week, the Netherlands was the last country to ratify NATO's enlargement to include the three former Warsaw Pact states.
The three are expected to join NATO in April to mark the 50th anniversary.
If their membership is secured, they can also participate in the planning for the NATO summit in April.
Sharping said at the end of a two-day visit that Poland was well prepared to join the alliance.
Germany has strongly advocated Poland's accession to NATO and said it would serve European security and stability.
Preliminary DNA tests link a missing abortion opponent to a strand of hair found near where a sniper shot dead a Buffalo, New York, doctor performing abortions, a law enforcement official said Friday.
The first round of DNA tests on the hair at the FBI lab here showed a high probability that it came from the same person as a hair found at a New Jersey home where James C. Kopp, a 44-year-old anti-abortion activist, lived last year, the official said.
Initial DNA tests did not rule out a match between the two strands.
Kopp has eluded authorities since they obtained an arrest warrant for him as a key witness in the October 23 shooting death of Dr. Barnett Slepian, a 52-year-old gynecologist and obstetrician who performed abortions.
The search for Kopp was recently expanded to Mexico.
Meanwhile, FBI agents and Metropolitan Police officers assigned to a joint terrorism task force searched the crowd of anti-abortion protesters at the annual March for Life on Capitol Hill because Kopp has either participated in or been arrested in each of the past three years, according to another law enforcement official.
Both officials requested anonymity.
The FBI is conducting further DNA tests on the hair found outside Dr. Slepian's home.
These more extensive tests could allow officials to determine an absolute match between the two strands of hair, the first law enforcement official said.
"Ultimately, we would have to have a hair removed by Kopp himself to conclude that the hair from the crime scene is his," the official added.
The official said the hair was in a cap that authorities found near the spot from which the sniper allegedly fired the bullet that killed Slepian as he stood in the kitchen of his home in the Buffalo suburb of Amherst, New York.
But The Buffalo News reported Friday that the hairs were in a package containing at least two bullets that was found buried more than 50 feet from a tree in the ground that police believe the sniper was standing and firing.
Last week, during an official visit to Mexico, FBI Director Louis Freeh asked Mexican authorities to join the hunt for Kopp, federal officials said.
Kopp was driven to Mexico by a friend after the shooting and had a trail of her credit card receipts leading to Mexico, federal authorities said.
A customs video from a border crossing also showed the woman's car returning from Mexico, officials said.
Authorities obtained a physical witness warrant against Kopp after they said Kopp's car was discovered in Amherst in the weeks before Slepian's death.
Kopps' 1987 Chevrolet Cavalier was discovered last month at Newark, N.J., the FBI said.
The car had been in continuous service for about two weeks, said Greg Trevor, a spokesman for the Port Authority of New York and New Jersey.
The creator of an anti-abortion website seen as a threat to abortion providers says the decision to close the site is only a "temporary setback."
Neal Horsley said Friday he will seek a new Internet service for his "Nuremberg Files" page, which was at the center of a federal court case that led to a $107 million ruling this week in favor of abortion providers.
Horsley was not a defendant in the lawsuit, in which jurors in Portland, Oregon, ruled that such sites posed a threat to abortion providers.
MindSpring, which provided the Internet space for the site, shut it down on Thursday night for violating "our reasonable usage policies," spokesman Serge Clermont said.
The website contained the names and addresses of abortion doctors and showed photos of mutilated fetuses and drawings of dripping blood.
Doctors whose names appeared on the list have been killed three times, most recently last October when Dr. Barnett Slepian was gunned down by snipers at his home outside Buffalo, N.Y. His name on the website was crossed out the same day.
Pathway Communications, which maintains the site, said in a statement that "The Nuremberg Files" had been removed without notice.
The company said on Friday it had been informed that it was in breach of MindSpring's guidelines on "threatening and harassing language."
Horsley called the move a "temporary setback."
"We are currently looking for a bolder and more principled (Internet service provider) to host the Nuremberg files," the statement said.
Foreign Minister Madeleine Albright has invited the foreign ministers of Poland, the Czech Republic and Hungary to a ceremony in Independence, Mo., on 12 March to mark the accession of the three countries to the NATO alliance.
The ceremony will be held at the Truman Presidential Library.
The venue was chosen to honor President Truman's announcement of the founding of NATO 50 years ago, the State Department said.
Poland, Hungary, and the Czech Republic became full NATO members today, less than a decade after swapping communist rule for democracy and severing Cold War military ties with the Soviet Union.
Secretary of State Madeleine Albright received the admission papers of the foreign ministers of the three countries, which mark their formal accession to the 50-year-old alliance.
"Hallelujah," Albright shouted.
< unk > < unk > Hungary has come home.
We are back in the family, "said Hungarian Foreign Minister Janos Martonyi.
With a touch of symbolism, the ceremony took place at the Harry S. Truman Library.
During Truman's presidency, the North Atlantic Treaty Organization was established to stop the spread of Soviet-led communism westward.
Ironically, just a decade ago, NATO's three youngest members were under the Soviet umbrella.
Czech Foreign Minister Jan Kavan said he was in nearby Kansas City in 1968 to work with the anti-Soviet underground organization when Russian troops swept through Prague during the occupation of Czechoslovakia.
"It is very symbolic to me that right here today we accept the guarantee that my country will never again be the powerless victim of a foreign invasion," Kavan said.
With a view to even greater enlargement of NATO, Polish Foreign Minister Bronislaw Geremek said: "We should keep the door of the Alliance open to those who have fought for freedom...
Another curtain should never fall on Europe. ""
Since 1949 it has been extended to Greece and Turkey in 1952, Germany in 1955 and Spain in 1982.
The new arrivals bring the total to 19, and many more hopefuls are waiting in the wings: Slovenia, Romania, Bulgaria, Slovakia, Lithuania, Estonia and Latvia.
What exactly NATO will do with these eager applicants is unclear.
The issue is expected to dominate the NATO summit scheduled for April in Washington.
NATO's mission has changed dramatically since its founding in 1949, at a time when the West sought to halt the spread of Soviet-led communism westward - a threat that Winston Churchill warned of in his famous "Iron Curtain" speech on March 5, 1946, at Westminster College in Fulton, Mo., 130 miles east of here.
NATO warplanes helped end the war in Bosnia nearly four years ago and are now ready to intervene in Yugoslavia if peace efforts in Kosovo fail.
A decade ago, military planning in Poland, Hungary, and the Czech Republic was built around the possibility of a ground attack to the west.
As NATO allies, the goal is much smaller armies capable of handling a variety of contingencies.
The State Department said the three new members "strengthen NATO's ability to fulfill its core mission of collective defense, respond to a range of security challenges, and reduce the possibility of another major conflict in Europe that has claimed so many American lives."
NATO Secretary General Javier Solana says: "The accession of the Czech Republic, Hungary and Poland... is perhaps the clearest evidence that Europe is growing closer together."
"Extending membership to these three democracies will help stabilise a region that has historically been the scene of many disasters this century."
But many Russians - and some Americans - are skeptical of NATO's push eastward.
Former Soviet leader Mikhail Gorbachev says the push east is a rejection of the common European security system discussed at the end of the Cold War.
Russian Foreign Minister Igor Ivanov calls the extension of NATO membership to Eastern Europe "a move in the wrong direction."
"All European states must work together to create a common security system," he was quoted as saying.
"All European states must work together in the interests of all countries and not in the interests of individual groups."
A rifle found near the home of a shot abortion doctor could provide important clues to investigators in the search for the shooter.
Dr. Barnett Slepian was killed by a sniper's bullet in his kitchen last fall.
Investigators said Friday they found a rifle near his home in the Buffalo suburb of Amherst.
The weapon was sent to the FBI lab in Washington to determine if it was used to kill Slepian.
"If it's not too rusty, maybe they can compare the bullet to the gun again," said Walter Rowe, a forensics professor at George Washington University.
The gun could also have clothing fibers, fingerprints or a serial number that can be traced back to the buyer, Rowe said.
The weapon, which was buried a foot underground, was found Thursday during a search of a heavily wooded, four-acre area.
Investigators did not say how close the gun was to the house.

An anti-abortion activist who is the subject of an international manhunt was charged Thursday with murder for killing a doctor who was struck down by a sniper bullet in his kitchen.
James C. Kopp, 44, of St. Albans, Vt.
His whereabouts are unknown.
"We probably have reason to believe that this man, James Kopp, is responsible for the death of Dr. Barnett Slepian," FBI agent Bernard Tolbert said.
Investigators have been searching for Kopp since shortly after the killing on October 23.
Until Thursday, however, they had described him only as a key witness wanted for questioning - and not as a suspect.
In addition to a murder charge, Kopp also faces charges of violating the law on free access to hospital entrances by using lethal force against an abortion doctor.
Both counts carry a maximum sentence of life in prison.
The federal indictment also carries a fine of up to $250,000.
The newly filed charges would intensify the ongoing international manhunt, Tolbert said.
In response to the new allegations, Canadian officials confirmed that Kopp is a suspect in sniper shootings that injured abortion doctors in Vancouver, British Columbia, Winnipeg, Manitoba and Hamilton, Ontario, in November.
Investigators would not disclose the evidence against Kopp, but said the discovery last month of a remote-controlled rifle buried near the Slepian house represented a major breakthrough.
The 52-year-old Slepian was shot with a rifle.

Kopp, nicknamed the "nuclear dog" in anti-abortion circles, had been arrested in several states since 1990 for protesting against abortion.
His car was discovered in the weeks before the shooting in suburban Amherst and found abandoned at Newark International Airport in New Jersey in December, investigators said.
Kopp's stepmother, who married Kopp's father when Kopp was in his 30s, said Thursday from her home in Irving, Texas, "I would like him to come forward and say his name if he's not guilty, and if he's guilty, contact a priest and make amends with society, then face what he did."

The FBI on Monday placed suspected terrorist mastermind Osama Bin Laden and abortion opponent and suspected doctor-killer James Charles Kopp on its list of the 10 most wanted fugitives.
FBI Director Louis Freeh said the U.S. government is offering a reward of up to $5 million for information leading to the arrest and conviction of Bin Laden, who is accused of masterminding the two bombings of American embassies in Kenya and Tanzania.
On 7 August 1998, more than 200 people, including 12 Americans, were killed in bombings.
It is the biggest reward the government has ever offered for a refugee.
Meanwhile, a total of $650,000 is being offered for information leading to the arrest of Kopp, who is accused of shooting Dr. Barnett Slepian last fall at his home in Buffalo, New York. Slepian provided legal abortions in the western state of New York.
The standard $50,000 reward for information leading to the capture of a listed fugitive comes on top of a $500,000 reward previously announced by Attorney General Janet Reno and a $100,000 reward previously offered by the FBI.
Reno said Bin Laden and Kopp had one thing in common: Each of them wrongly believed that it was justified to use all the means at his disposal to achieve his goal.
They also have common places unknown to the authorities.
Freeh said Bin Laden may be hiding in Afghanistan, which has been uncooperative in the case.
Kopp had approached the border with Mexico, but it was not known if he had entered the country, Freeh said.

The FBI continues to receive threats from individuals and organizations with ties to Bin Laden, Freeh said.
Carpenter said the U.S. government's counterterrorism program has paid more than $6 million for credible information in about 20 cases worldwide.

The two were removed from the list after Libyan leader Muammar Gaddafi agreed in April to try them in the Netherlands, a neutral place.

U.S. Secretary of State Madeleine Albright traveled to East Africa on Tuesday to show American support in the wake of the bombings of U.S. embassies that killed mostly African victims.
"To the American embassy staff in Nairobi and Dar es Salaam, I will convey a message of solidarity in their grief, admiration for their courage and support for their continued efforts on behalf of our country," Albright said Monday before leaving Andrews Air Force Base in Maryland.
"I will bring a message of friendship to the people of Kenya and Tanzania, deepened by our shared grief, and support to do everything in our power to alleviate their suffering and help them begin a new beginning."
After a 16-hour flight, Albright is scheduled to spend just five hours on the ground in Dar es Salaam, Tanzania, her first stop Tuesday morning, and then about five and a half hours in Nairobi, Kenya, later in the day before returning home immediately.
Albright's visit to East Africa was intended in part to demonstrate US resolve in the face of growing terrorist threats to Americans.
Some Kenyans have complained that after the Nairobi bombing, rescuers prioritized finding American victims and protecting evidence at the expense of saving African lives.
But Albright and other U.S. officials have vehemently denied that notion, saying the priority has been to rescue all manner of people, African and American.
On Friday, as Albright withdrew his vacation plans for a hastily planned trip to East Africa, the State Department lifted a travel warning for Kenya, whose economy relies heavily on foreign visitors to further ease the burden after the tragedy.
Just before Albright's one-day trip, the State Department issued an updated "global warning" to U.S. travelers on Sunday following the August 7 bombings of American embassies in Kenya and Tanzania and new threats to U.S. interests abroad.
An accompanying statement explicitly warned against "travelling to Pakistan."
The State Department also ordered the departure of all staff and families of embassy staff in Islamabad and U.S. consulates in Karachi, Lahore and Peshawar.
The warnings came after Pakistan handed over a suspect in the bombings of the U.S. Embassy, Mohammed Saddiq Odeh, also known as Mohammad Sadiq Howaida and Abdull Bast Awadh, to Kenyan officials.
The first batch of evidence from the Nairobi bombing arrived at the FBI lab at its Washington headquarters over the weekend and is being analyzed, FBI officials said Monday.
Many more deliveries are expected, but the first batch contained cotton buds taken from the blast site in hopes of picking up microscopic residues that could clearly identify what the bomb was made of.
A U.S. official said last week that field tests indicated the presence of a Czech-made plastic explosive called Semtex, which has already been used by terrorist groups.

The U.S. Immigration and Naturalization Service had ruled that Elian must be returned to his father, Juan Miguel Gonzalez, and set Friday as the deadline for his return to Cuba.

Ricardo Alarcon, president of the National Assembly and Castro's point of contact for U.S.-Cuban relations, told The Associated Press that Cuban authorities were frustrated by Reno's failure to set a new deadline and the INS's failure to enforce its decision.
"The INS has never announced an enforcement action," he said.
Alarcon, a former Cuban ambassador to the United Nations, rejected suggestions by some U.S. politicians and Elian's relatives in Miami that Elian's father should travel from Cardenas, Cuba, to Miami to pick up the boy.
He said Cuba had not banned Gonzalez from traveling to Miami to retrieve Elian, and American lawyers - and even American officials - had advised against it.
"We have received the same message from U.S. officials - privately - several times that it is not advisable for this man to appear on U.S. territory," Alarcon said.

Canadian authorities on Monday issued a nationwide arrest warrant for James Charles Kopp, a U.S. fugitive on the FBI's wanted list for an alleged murder of anti-abortion activists.
Kopp is also wanted for at least two other shootings of doctors who performed abortions.
The Canadian arrest warrant issued Monday charges Kopp with attempted murder in the 1995 shooting of Dr. Hugh Short, who was wounded in the elbow but survived the attack at his home in Ancaster, Ontario.
"It allows us to spread his name across Canada and say he is wanted for attempted murder," said Police Inspector Dave Bowen.
Kopp, who is from Vermont, was charged last year with fatally shooting Dr. Barnet Slepian, an obstetrician in Buffalo, New York, in October 1998.
He is also wanted in connection with the shootings of doctors in Winnipeg, Vancouver and Rochester, New York.
Slepian was killed by a single shot fired from a wooded area through a window of his home.
Kopp claims to be a devout Catholic, and the majority of the jobs he has held in the past 20 years have been related to the anti-abortion movement, the U.S. Justice Department said last year.
He has been arrested more than a dozen times during abortion protests in seven states and Italy.
The FBI says Kopp used 29 aliases.
He was the 456th person to be placed on the FBI's wanted list.
Since then, 427 fugitives have been taken into custody or tracked down, 133 of them for citizen assistance, the FBI said.
A total of $650,000 is being offered in the US for information leading to Kopps' arrest.

On Friday, Cuba's communist government celebrated the birth of independence hero Jose Marti with rallies calling for the return of Elian Gonzalez, the "martyr of the boy" at the center of an international custody dispute.
Tens of thousands of children flocked to Havana's Revolution Square to pay tribute to Marti and Elian, the six-year-old Cuban whose rescue off the Florida coast two months ago sparked a tug-of-war between relatives on both sides of the Florida Strait.
In their red neckerchiefs of the communist pioneers, a number of boys and girls recited poems they had written for Elian, described as Cuba's "boy hero" and "symbolic child," and for Marti, commonly known on the island as Cuba's "teacher" and "apostle."
The Communist Party newspaper Granma compared Elian to revolutionary icon Ernesto < unk > Che'Guevara and said the boy < unk > "has forever become a symbol of the crimes and injustices that imperialism can commit against an innocent person."
The commemorations were similar to those held annually here for Marti, the revolutionary and poet who fought for Cuba's independence from Spain in 1898.
But this year, the children wore T-shirts with Elian's likeness and school uniforms.
The commemorations began early Friday morning, as tens of thousands of Cubans marched through the streets of Havana under a torch.

President Fidel Castro said Tuesday that his government is concerned that Miami exiles could take Elian Gonzalez to another country or even harm him if they lose their fight against the boy's return to Cuba.
Castro, quoting Cuban intelligence documents on state television, said a group of Cuban Americans in Miami was exploring the possibility of taking Elian and his relatives from Miami to a third nation, probably Nicaragua or Costa Rica.
Castro said his government had shared its concerns with the State Department about the possibility of such a move "that could harm not only a child but also the reputation of the United States."
A State Department official said she had no information about a threat to Elian and was unaware of Castro's remarks.
In Miami, the boy's great uncle, Lazaro Gonzalez, denied that he would send Elian out of the country or leave himself.
"It never crossed my mind," he said.
The Cuban leader first raised those concerns during another live television appearance Sunday night during a speech to about 700 Cuban university students.
But it wasn't until Tuesday that Castro revealed that those concerns had been shared directly with the U.S. government.
Tens of thousands of people paid tribute on Saturday to the men who sparked the Cuban revolution more than four decades ago at a rally calling for the return of Elian Gonzalez.
With the historic Granma yacht behind him, Raul Castro, younger brother of President Fidel Castro and head of Cuba's Revolutionary Armed Forces, likened the revolutionary battle to the battle for Elian, the six-year-old boy at the center of an international custody dispute.
"" Nobody is giving up here! ""
Castro said.
The demonstrators wore T-shirts bearing Elian's portrait and waved red, white and blue Cuban flags on the shore of the municipality of Niquero, where the Granma landed on December 2, 1956, with 82 armed men led by Fidel Castro.
In the following years, the guerrillas waged war against the troops of the dictator Fulgencio Batista and declared victory on 1 January 1959, when Batista fled the country.
Castro pointed to the importance Cuba's younger generations have played in the communist government's struggle to get Elian back from the United States, where the boy's relatives are fighting for permanent custody in Miami.
A father and grandfather of eight children - including a boy who just turned 6, the same age as Elian - Castro said he sympathized with the little boy's father, Juan Miguel Gonzalez.
Gonzalez cited his rights as the only surviving parent of the child in his demands for his boy to be returned to him in communist Cuba.
Elian's mother and 10 other people were killed when their boat sank off the Florida coast in late November during a crossing from Cuba to the United States.
'Today, Elian is the symbol of our unity, 'Castro said to cheers.
'And our struggle will not end with the return of Elian, 'Castro added.
He said the Cuban people would continue to protest against American laws and measures aimed at Cuba, including a four-decade economic embargo and the continued U.S. military presence at the Guantanamo naval base at the eastern end of the island.
In an apparent attempt to show flexibility in the campaign to return Elian Gonzalez to Cuba, President Fidel Castro said the boy's father had offered to travel alone to the United States today to pick up his 6-year-old son.
However, a group trip with the boy's relatives and classmates seemed more likely than a solo trip by his father, Juan Miguel González.
Gonzalez, the boy's stepmother, his little half-brother, a dozen classmates and some adults were planning to apply for American visas today at the U.S. Interests Section in Havana, the U.S. mission here.
The group is ready to leave for the United States as early as Tuesday, Castro said.
Gonzalez said he would leave his son alone if U.S. authorities promised to extradite him immediately and allow him to return to Cuba immediately, Castro said Sunday.
"I am ready to go all alone tomorrow and go where the child is," Castro read from a letter signed by González.
"Only the government of the United States can decide whether it prefers this option," the letter, which Castro read on state television, read.
Gonzalez denied for months requests from Elian's relatives in Miami to travel to the United States to claim the boy.
At the same time, lawyers for the boy's relatives in Miami said they could try to argue that his father was an inappropriate parent.
The White House said there was no evidence of wrongdoing, and the father's lawyer, Gregory Craig, said the "outrageous" allegations were a sign that relatives in Miami were desperate.
It was unclear whether the American authorities would issue the 28 visas they proposed to Cubans.
The group would not leave until Gonzalez had assurances that American authorities would give him custody of his son.
It is planned that he and other members of the delegation in Washington will remain with Cuban diplomats until the results of an appeal by Elian's relatives in Miami, who are taking him into temporary custody and trying to prevent his return to Cuba.
In another dramatic move, Castro announced that the head of the Cuban Interest Section in Washington was willing to waive diplomatic immunity at his residence, where Cuba has proposed that González stay with Elian and the rest of his family during the federal appeals process - which could take up to a month or two.
Castro said that if those who oppose Elian's return to Cuba are worried about handing the child over to "what is considered Cuban territory, then our advocacy group is willing to waive the diplomatic immunity of the head of that department in Washington."
Elian has been at the center of an international tug of war since he was rescued off the coast of Florida in late November.
His mother was among 11 people who died after their boat sank during an attempted crossing from Cuba to the United States.
Elian's relatives in Miami are fighting to keep the boy in the United States, saying they could give him a better life off the communist island.
Gonzalez has claimed his rights as the only surviving parent of the child and wants Elian returned to him.
In its latest deadline, the U.S. Immigration and Naturalization Service has set a 9 a.m. deadline for relatives in Miami.
Tuesday to sign a paper promising to hand the boy over to immigration authorities if they lose their appeal in federal court.
Relatives in Miami have so far refused to sign the document, saying in recent days that they would not extradite Elian to his father if he came to the United States.
They had previously said they would give the boy Gonzalez if he made the trip.
Before his speech Sunday night, Castro played the loving grandfather to the 12 first-graders who planned to go on the trip, assuring them that their planned trip to Washington to bring back their little friend was a very important mission.
Castro patted some of the children on the head and assured them that they had formed a powerful "command."
"Not even the Pentagon can handle these kids," he joked.
"" We hope they get their visas. ""
President Fidel Castro said Wednesday night that it was only a matter of days before Elian Gonzalez would be reunited with his father.
"I'm talking about three days," Castro told hundreds of students from across Latin America and the Caribbean gathered in the city for a regional congress.
'The battle of Elian is won - legally, legally, politically, 'Castro said at the end of his speech at the Karl Marx Theater in Havana.
Castro's statement came less than an hour after Gregory Craig, the lawyer for Elian's father, Juan Miguel Gonzalez, announced in Washington that the father would travel to the United States on Thursday with his wife, their 6-month-old son and Fernando Remirez, the head of the Cuban Interests Division in Washington.
'This brave father will leave at 4am (Thursday 4am EDT), 'Castro said.
"" And I at least will be there to say goodbye to him. ""
He said the group would be greeted on arrival in Washington by Craig and Joan Brown Campbell of the National Council of Churches, who traveled with Craig on his lightning trip to Havana on Wednesday.
The Cuban leader said the U.S. government still did not know how it would hand Elian over to his father, but that it would do so quickly.
The U.S. Immigration and Customs Enforcement said earlier this week that once Gonzalez arrived in the United States, it would begin the process of transferring Elian to his custody by relatives in Miami who have been looking after him since his rescue off the Florida coast in late November.
The Miami-based family is fighting to keep him in the United States, saying it can give him a better life off the communist island.
But González, backed by Castro, has claimed his rights as Elian's only surviving parent, insisting that the child be returned to him.
Elian's mother was killed along with 10 others when her boat sank while crossing from Cuba to the United States.
Since the tragedy, Elian has become a political figurehead for Cubans with diametrically opposed ideologies living on both sides of the Florida Strait.
During his speech Wednesday night, Castro expressed anger that much of the rest of the delegation was not granted visas to travel to the United States to stay with Elian while he awaited a federal application filed by Elian's relatives in Miami to help with his "rehabilitation."
"These 12 children are more important than the doctors," Castro said from Elian's first class in Cuba.
Cuba had applied for 28 visas for the trip, for Elian's father, his stepmother, his little half-brother, a fifth-grade cousin, the dozen classmates and an army of teachers, doctors and other specialists.
Also on the list was National Assembly President Ricardo Alarcon, who served as an adviser to González for the past four months and was a senior member of Cuba's communist leadership.
In the end, the US authorities approved and issued six visas for the four family members, a pediatrician and Elian's teacher from his kindergarten year.
After a weekend of bitter accusations from both sides in the custody dispute over Elian Gonzalez, attention returned today to the federal judges who ruled a key point in the international case.
The 11th U.S. Circuit Court of Appeals in Atlanta has been asked by the U.S. government to stay a ruling by one of its judges that prevents Elian from being deported from the country.
Such a decision would allow the US government to push for a swift reunion of the six-year-old boy in Washington with his waiting father, Juan Miguel Gonzalez, who wants to return to Cuba.
He has said he will await appeals from relatives in Miami seeking an asylum hearing for Elian in the same court - but only if he has custody of his son.
Concern grew outside the Little Havana home where the boy has lived with his great-uncle for nearly five months.
"The feeling is that the Clinton administration could send federal agents to pull him out of here," said Sergio Navarro, a 39-year-old Miami businessman who spent part of Sunday night with chanting protesters.
Elian's relatives have been looking after him since November, when he was found hanging from a hose in the Florida street.
His mother and 10 other people fleeing Cuba drowned when their boat sank.
The Clinton administration has pushed for the boy to return to his father, ruling that only he can speak for his son on immigration issues.
Relatives of the Miami boy say he will have a better life here and have tried to portray Juan Miguel Gonzalez as Fidel Castro's henchman.
The Justice Department wants the appeals court to overturn the restraining order issued Thursday and also order Elian's great uncle Lazaro Gonzalez to release the boy.
The relatives want the court to allow them to meet with Elian's father without having to extradite the boy.
The trial follows one of the most bitter charges to date in the custody dispute.
Juan Miguel Gonzalez lashed out at his relatives in Miami Sunday night, accusing them of "child abuse" for turning his son against him.
He said they had manipulated his son into believing that his mother might one day show up - in this country.
"This is child abuse and abuse, what they're doing to this boy," Gonzalez said on CBS's "60 Minutes." "The way they're abusing him, turning him against his father... he's suffering more here than in the sea." "
Gonzalez said he did not believe a widely publicized video taken at the relatives' home in which his son said he did not want to go.
Since arriving in the United States on April 6, he has spoken to Elian at least three times and insisted that his son wants to return to Cuba.
"He told me that," Gonzalez said.
Relatives in Miami are laying a pile of toys in front of a six-year-old.
He cannot decide for himself.
Whoever chooses him is me, his father. ""
Elian's relatives in Miami have filed affidavits accusing Gonzalez of abusing his ex-wife and 6-year-old son, which Gonzalez denies.
During Sunday's broadcast, family members and Elian were outside Lazaro Gonzalez's home and did not speak publicly after the broadcast.
Protesters, however, dismissed Juan Miguel's comments as either selfish or Fidel Castro's words.
"Fidel Castro claims the son, not him," said Olga Coto, 56, of Miami.
'He's completely manipulated by Fidel Castro.
He's not telling the truth. ""
Many feared that their efforts to keep the boy in Miami would fail today, on the 39th anniversary of the Bay of Pigs fiasco, a failed invasion of Cuba by CIA-trained exiles that left much bitterness in the Cuban-American community.
"We were betrayed by the government, by (President) Kennedy, and if they return the child, it will be a second betrayal," said Josefa González, who left Cuba in 1962 when she was four years old.
Early Sunday, at the start of Easter week, demonstrators prayed for divine help and waved palm fronds and placards depicting the Cuban boy and Jesus.
Priests joined the crowd for a service.
"There are a lot of people telling us to give up this fight," said Martin Anorga, an evangelical pastor.
"" But we will follow the way that the Lord has shown us, despite those who want to get in our way. ""
The bombings of the American embassies in Kenya and Tanzania spooked travelers from East Africa, just as Kenya hoped for an end to the years-long slump in foreign tourism, and when Tanzania finally began to benefit from a major investment in the travel industry.
Tour operators specialising in travel in Kenya and Tanzania, which have some of Africa's best wildlife parks and sunniest beaches, say there have been no mass cancellations following the bombings in the Kenyan capital Nairobi on 7 August and in Dar es Salaam, Tanzania, which killed more than 250 people.
But they report a series of concerned calls from potential travelers, many of whom are alarmed by the possibility of further terrorist attacks in the region.
"People have called and we have assured them that we don't see any further problems," said Mary Mkimbo, a spokeswoman for Kenya's tourism office in New York.
The US State Department, which does not want Kenya and Tanzania to be economically punished for terrorist attacks clearly aimed at the United States, has lifted the dire travel warnings issued just after the bombings, which urged Americans to stay away from the two countries.
New public announcements on August 13 only suggest that Americans are "exercising caution" when visiting Kenya and Tanzania.
Embassy staff in both cities have resumed work, albeit at new locations.
The bombed-out embassy in Nairobi is expected to be demolished after the FBI completes its evidence collection.
The embassy in Dar es Salaam was less damaged by the detonation, although it may also have to be razed to the ground.
Currently, the State Department says, embassy staff can only provide consular emergency services.
Western diplomats and intelligence officials say there is no reason to believe that terrorists linked to Osama bin Laden, the exiled Saudi millionaire accused of orchestrating the embassy bombings, will strike again soon in both countries.
"The tourism industry is very sensitive to the media," Mkimbo said.
"We expect a slight downward movement in the industry." "
Tourism revenue reportedly fell to $300 million last year, compared with $450 million the year before.
The government has responded to the slump with a series of measures to lure back tourists, including a significant improvement in service from Kenya Airways, the national carrier that has partnered with KLM Royal Dutch Airlines.
The bombing will undoubtedly set these efforts back.
But the lack of infrastructure has worked in Tanzania's favor in recent years, as the number of adventure travelers willing, or even willing, to forgo Kenya's five-star safari resorts has risen dramatically.
The number of visitors to Tanzania rose from less than 100,000 in 1980 to more than 300,000 last year, prompting the government to improve the roads needed for safari tours.
"Tanzania has been underdeveloped for years, and they are now starting to develop it," said Jalal Ebrahim, president of Safaricentre International, a tour operator in Manhattan Beach, Calif.
Ebrahim, whose company has been sending American travelers on safaris in East Africa for 13 years, said his company had received only one cancellation as a result of the embassy bombings and that he did not expect many more.
"We had a cancellation from a couple who decided to just wait and see what happens," he said.
They read a lot, they stay up to date on world affairs. ""
They are aware that terrorism increasingly knows no borders.
The New York Times wrote in an editorial on Monday, October 26:
The murder of Dr Barnett Slepian is the latest depraved attack on the small number of brave doctors who still risk their safety to perform legal abortions in this country.
Slepian was one of only three doctors performing abortions in the Buffalo, New York area, and harassment and threats against him by abortion opponents had become routine over the past decade.
He insisted on courage and belief in the right of his patients to decide for themselves whether they want to have a child.
But a basic commitment to a constitutionally protected service could not stop the assassin's bullet that smashed through his kitchen window and into his back Friday night.
His death shows once again how timid the right to abortion has become in the face of terrorism by anti-choice fanatics.
Their repeated acts of terrorism must be dealt with as harshly as possible by the law enforcement authorities.
If an armed police officer must be stationed outside the home and office of every abortion provider 24 hours a day, then so be it.
Last week, the FBI joined Canadian authorities in investigating a series of unsolved shootings of doctors by snipers in Canada and western New York in 1994.
In January, a week after the 25th anniversary of the Supreme Court's Roe v. Wade decision, a security guard was killed and a nurse seriously injured in a bomb attack on a clinic in Birmingham, Alabama.
Since 1993, three doctors, including Slepian, three hospital employees and a clinic attendant have been murdered.
There have been more than a dozen attempted murders of abortion providers in the past five years.
Arson attacks and bombings of clinics are now so widespread that many no longer make the national news.
It is bad enough that conservatives in Congress and state legislatures are working to dismantle reproductive rights by banning certain procedures, such as so-called partial-birth abortion, and requiring waiting times and parental consent before an abortion can be performed.
But these restrictions are at least imposed by the normal give and take of political and judicial struggle in a democracy.
What is outrageous is the attempt to stop abortions by illegal means, by shooting doctors, bombing clinics, or harassing women who wish to exercise their constitutional right.
Some abortion opponents have condemned the bombings and shootings, but others seem reluctant to issue the harsh condemnations that are warranted.
Indeed, one particularly virulent anti-abortion website lists the names of doctors who are said to perform abortions or "crimes against humanity," "with a code indicating whether they are" working, "" wounded, "or" lethal. ""
Such inflammatory rhetoric, and the frequent accusations by some abortion opponents that abortion providers are committing murder, can only fuel terrorism.
The proliferation of anti-choice laws and sustained harassment campaigns by protest groups, even on the verge of murder, have helped make abortions harder to obtain.
More than 80 percent of counties in this country do not have an abortion provider.
In 45 states, the number of doctors performing abortions declined between 1982 and 1992.
Slepian was among those who spoke out against the restriction of reproductive freedom and paid with their lives for it.
Unless these brave doctors are better protected, the next generation of doctors will be less willing to put themselves and their families at risk.
That's what the terrorists want.
They must not be allowed to succeed.
Further details of the killing were released on Sunday.
Slepian's lawyer Glenn Edward Murray said the sniper's bullet entered through a partially draped kitchen window, hit Slepian in the back and came out through his chest.
The doctor's wife and one of his sons were in the kitchen at the time of the shooting, Murray said.
The bullet bounced off a closet and hit the living room, where the doctor's three other children were watching TV, Murray said.
Slepian's wife and son tried to resuscitate him.
He was pronounced dead at nearby Millard Fillmore Suburban Hospital just over an hour later.
Most people on both sides of the abortion issue expressed outrage at the shooting.
But one abortion opponent, the Rev. Donald Spitz, the founder of Pro-Life Virginia, called Slepian's killer a "hero" for "ending the doctor's bloodthirsty practice."
"As Christians, we have a responsibility to protect the innocent from being murdered," Spitz said in a statement to The Associated Press, "just as we would like someone to protect us."
Whoever fired the shot was protecting the children. ""
Most other abortion opponents, however, condemned the shooting and abortion.
In his Sunday Mass at St. Patrick's Cathedral in New York, Cardinal John O'Connor called the killing false, but he urged those angered by the killing to be just as angry about abortion.
"He called on many of these people to express their outrage at the partial abortion, which he called infanticide," Joseph Zwilling, a spokesman for the Archdiocese of New York, said of the cardinal's sermon.
Religious leaders in Buffalo also condemned the shooting.
Bishop Henry Mansell of the Archdiocese of Buffalo called it an "act of madness," but defended the church's opposition to abortion as "respect for human life in all its forms, especially for the most vulnerable."
Susan Dudley, deputy director of the National Abortion Federation, attacked the remarks and called on abortion opponents to change their tactics.
She said the anti-abortion rhetoric had contributed to violent attacks on abortion providers.
"I think when pro-life organizations try to distinguish between themselves and these incidents, they are disingenuous at best," she said.
"They're what these doctors call murderers." "
In Amherst, residents who opposed abortion condemned the attack and said they hoped the perpetrator would be caught.
Men who smoked cigars and drank beer with friends after shooting at the Buffalo Shooting Club in Amherst Skeet said their feelings about abortion were mixed, but they uniformly condemned the killing of Slepian.
Many residents said abortion has gradually declined as a local issue since the anti-abortion group Operation Rescue launched a series of largely unsuccessful "Spring of Life" demonstrations in 1992 aimed at closing abortion clinics and harassing local doctors, including Slepian.
Little remains of the bitterness that the issue evoked in the late 1980s and early 1990s, residents said, and many of the local religious leaders who led the 1992 protests have moved.
"The only people left are the hard-boiled, fanatical people," said Larry Finkelstien, a high school teacher who works as an escort at abortion clinics.
"Everyone else has gone." "
The killing of Dr. Barnett Slepian, a Buffalo gynecologist who performed abortions, has become a factor in at least two campaigns in New York, political consultants and some campaign advisers say.
In the race for attorney general, Democratic candidate Eliot Spitzer, who supports abortion rights, has moved quickly to make the anti-abortion position of his opponent, Attorney General Dennis Vacco, a central issue in the campaign.
And while Democrat Charles Schumer, another abortion-rights advocate, did not bring up Slepian's death himself in the tight US Senate race, his aides say it adds a new emphasis to the issue.
Slepian was shot in the back as he stood in his kitchen on Friday night.
No suspects have been identified.
Even before the assassination, Schumer was trying to draw more attention to the anti-abortion record of his opponent, Senator Alfonse D'Amato, a Republican who is also running on the Right to Life Party line.
In a New York Times / CBS News poll completed Saturday, 69 percent of respondents said they did not know D'Amato's position on abortion, and only 19 percent knew he opposed abortion rights.
Now events are getting the job done for Schumer.
Slepian's death was one of the first topics raised in Saturday night's debate between the two men, and D'Amato was immediately asked if he opposed first-trimester abortions for adult women.
On Tuesday, the National Abortion and Reproductive Rights Action League plans to hold a news conference to show a television ad made last week before Slepian died - featuring Emily Lyons, a nurse who was seriously injured in the bombing of an Alabama abortion clinic earlier this year.
The ads, said Kelli Conlin, executive director of Naral, a New York state affiliate, are intended to highlight D'Amato's vote against the Schumer-sponsored Freedom of Access to Clinic Entrances Act of 1994.
The measure made it a federal crime to attack or block an abortion clinic.
Now, Conlin said, it will find new resonance and the organization is considering buying more airtime.
"It's incredibly sincere and powerful, and it wouldn't have mattered," she said.
'But the sensitivities are so heightened by the shooting in western New York that it's even more powerful. '
David Eichenbaum, a Democratic political consultant who is not working for anyone in this year's campaign, said abortion is "no longer the driving issue it once was, at least on the side of pro-choice advocates."
But, Eichenbaum said, "Sometimes an incident like this does more to motivate those who are pro-choice, who might not otherwise see it as an important issue to go to the polls on.
She's bringing it home. ""
D'Amato's aides did not comment on the political implications of the assassination, and Schumer's aides did not comment on whether they intended to mention it in any ads or whether the candidate intended to use it in the campaign.
"Both Senator D'Amato and Chuck Schumer agree that this was a terrible crime and a terrible tragedy," said Josh Isay, Schumer's campaign manager.
But, Isay added, "it exacerbates the abortion problem."
Steven Goldstein, Spitzer's campaign spokesman, was less restrained.
"Eliot will make that a huge priority," Goldstein said.
"Ending violence in clinics will be a key theme of our campaign from now until Election Day," he said.
In Sunday's debate against Vacco, Spitzer attacked him for failing to enforce a 1992 court order against abortion opponents who had blocked the entrance to a clinic.
It was the same clinic where Slepian worked.
A federal judge removed Vacco, then the U.S. attorney for western New York, from the case.
He was replaced by two special prosecutors.
Spitzer also criticized Vacco for dismantling the Office of Reproductive Rights, which investigated threats and crimes against abortion clinics when he became attorney general.
On Monday, Spitzer called on Vacco to immediately revive the unit, promising to do so on his first day in office if elected.
Goldstein, who said it was "thoroughly, thoroughly possible" that the issue appeared in Spitzer's ads, defended the use of the issue, saying the attorney general had direct influence over the issue of clinic access.
Vacco's campaign manager, Matt Behrmann, said in a statement that Spitzer had "sunk to a new and despicable low by trying to capitalize on the murder of a doctor to win votes."
"It is possible that New York politics has never seen anything as stark as Eliot Spitzer's willingness to use the unfortunate murder of Slepian as a springboard for his own political ambitions," Berhrmann said.
Several advisers agreed that there might be political hay to be made from Slepian's death, but that it had to be done carefully.
"Trying to score political points with a murder can trigger a backlash," said Maureen Connelly, a media consultant who does not work for any candidate in next week's election.
The anti-abortion activist who was to be questioned after the shooting of a doctor in a Buffalo suburb last month may have fled to Mexico, according to a newspaper report.
James Charles Kopp, 44, of Vermont, was seen in the car of a co-worker crossing the U.S. border, days after a sniper killed Dr. Barnett Slepian in his kitchen, the Buffalo News reported Friday.
Kopp was the subject of a manhunt after it was reported that he was discovered in a vehicle near the scene of the killing of Slepian, 52, an obstetrician who also performed abortions.
The FBI says Kopp is wanted as a key witness in the case.
An FBI official said Friday that authorities were looking into more than 400 leads but had not determined Kopps' whereabouts.
"In all honesty, the FBI does not know where James Kopp is," said Paul Moskal, a spokesman for the Buffalo office.
'He'll probably be in Boston, Massachusetts, as well as Buffalo, New York. '
Authorities want to question the unidentified woman, who is believed to have been traveling with Kopp, the newspaper reported, citing an investigator.
"She may have been with Kopp at some point after the murder," the investigator said.
President Bill Clinton promised Saturday to hunt down and bring to justice those responsible for the bombings of two U.S. embassies in Kenya and Tanzania.
"No matter how long it takes or where it takes us, we will pursue terrorists until the cases are resolved and justice is done," Clinton said in his weekly radio address.
"Turning our backs on those who take risks for peace, weakening our resistance to terrorism - that would give terrorism a victory that it cannot and will not have," the president said.
Friday's explosions at the U.S. embassies in Nairobi, Kenya, and Dar es Salaam, Tanzania, killed at least 110 people and wounded more than 2,200.
The 11 Americans and five Kenyan embassy employees were killed in Kenya.
The U.S. State Department has sent medical and other supplies as well as investigators to the two countries.
The FBI also sent dozens of agents and bomb and evidence specialists there.
Planes carrying medical equipment and backup personnel for the two embassies have taken off in the United States, Europe and elsewhere in Africa.
A 62-member rescue team sent to the bombed-out federal building in Oklahoma City was on its way to Nairobi.
US embassies and military installations around the world have been ordered to erect barriers and tighten security systems to prevent easy access for unauthorized persons - Americans and foreigners.
The Cypriot government on Friday strongly condemned two terrorist attacks in Kenya and Tanzania, calling them a "heinous crime."
In a telegram of condolence to US President Bill Clinton, President Glafcos Clerides condemned the terrorist attacks and expressed his condolences to the families of the victims.
Similar telegrams were sent by the Cypriot government to the presidents of Kenya and Tanzania.
More than 100 people have died, including eight Americans, and more than 1,000 have been injured since a bomb ripped through an office building next to the U.S. Embassy in Nairobi, Kenya, on Friday morning.
A car bomb attack outside the U.S. Embassy in the Tanzanian capital Dar-es-Salaam on Friday also killed six people and wounded at least 58.
No one has claimed responsibility for the attacks.
The Cuban government on Friday condemned the two bomb explosions near the U.S. embassies in Kenya and Tanzania.
The State Department said in a statement that the Cuban government opposes such terrorist attacks, which pose potential threats to any country.
The Cuban government also expressed its condolences to the families of the victims.
Two bombs exploded outside the U.S. embassies in Kenya and Tanzania on Friday, killing at least 81 people and wounding more than 1,700 others.
At least 74 people, including eight Americans, were killed and more than 1,640 injured in Nairobi, Kenya's capital.
At least seven people have been reported dead and 72 others injured in the Tanzanian capital, Dar es Salaam.
Iran on Saturday condemned the bombing of the U.S. embassies in Kenya and Tanzania and expressed its condolences to the families of the victims.
Foreign Ministry spokesman Mahmoud Mohammadi said Iran condemns any actions that could lead to the death of innocent people, the official IRNA news agency reported.
He expressed the hope that "sinister terrorism" would be eradicated through a comprehensive campaign of international cooperation.
Explosions near the US embassies in Kenya and Tanzania killed more than 100 people and injured more than 1,000 others on Friday.
No one has claimed responsibility.
U.S. Secretary of State Madeleine Albright said Tuesday she was encouraged by Kenya and Tanzania's cooperation in investigating last Friday's bombings of U.S. embassies.
"I have been encouraged by the close cooperation with the authorities in Kenya and Tanzania since the time of the tragedies," Albright told ambassadors and chargé d'affaires from 30 African countries in Washington who came to the State Department to offer their condolences.
"I would like to thank you for the help your governments have provided in recent days by sharing information and tightening security at our embassies," she said.
The bombings of US embassies in Kenya and Tanzania killed more than 200 people, including 12 Americans, and wounded more than 5,000.
The United States has sent FBI agents to Africa to investigate the bombings, but there were no significant leads in Washington on Tuesday.
Tanzanian police said on Tuesday they had arrested 14 foreigners in the search for the attackers.
The 14 foreigners, six Iraqis, six Sudanese, one Somali and one Turk, were arrested because they could not satisfactorily explain their presence in the country.
Albright announced Monday that he would suspend a reward of up to $2 million for information leading to the arrest and conviction of the attackers.
She told the African envoys that she was looking forward to visiting Kenya and Tanzania, but would wait until she could do so without unduly burdening the heavily burdened U.S. embassies there.
She also said the bombings would not stop the United States from building "a strong new relationship with a new Africa."
Egyptian Ambassador Ahmed Maher el-Sayed, speaking on behalf of the African envoys, condemned the "cowardly, criminal and tragic" bombings.
"Terrorism is at odds with our African history, our tradition, our culture and our faith," he said.
"Americans and Africans must step up their cooperation in this universal fight" against terrorism.
President Bill Clinton on Thursday condemned terrorist attacks on U.S. embassies in Kenya and Tanzania and vowed to find the perpetrators and bring them to justice.
"No matter what it takes, we must find those responsible for these evil acts and ensure that justice is done," Clinton said at the ceremony marking the return of the remains of Americans killed in the bombing of the Kenyan embassy.
The ceremony took place at Andrews Air Force Base near Washington, where 10 of the 12 American victims of the bombing of the Kenyan embassy were returned by Secretary of State Madeleine Albright.
Clinton said the United States would not withdraw from the world because of terrorist attacks.
