A working group of US and Canadian officials is examining the similarities between the attack on Slepian and four others on abortion providers in Canada and New York state over the past four years.
The US Department of Justice has offered a $500,000 reward for information leading to the arrest and conviction of those responsible for Slepian's killing.
The FBI has interviewed and released one of two men wanted last week for questioning in connection with the killing of a doctor who performed abortions in Buffalo, New York.
The two men, Ronald Stauber and Michael Gingrich, were named Thursday in a bulletin sent by the FBI's Cleveland office to police departments across the country.
The men have not been named as suspects, and FBI officials on Saturday played down the likelihood that either of them was involved in the October 23 killing of doctor Barnett Slepian.
"We do dozens, if not hundreds, of interviews a day," said Paul Moskal, a spokesman for the FBI's Buffalo office.
'I'm not aware of any information that would treat these two differently or put them in any other category out of the hundreds of clues we examine every day. '
Moskal said one of the two men said he did not know which one had been located, questioned and released.
In its Friday edition, The Hamilton (Ontario) Spectator quoted an Amherst, N.Y., Police Department official as saying the FBI had hinted at possible links between the two men and James Charles Kopp, an abortion opponent wanted as a key witness in the case.
Amherst police declined to comment on Saturday.
Slepian, 52, an obstetrician and gynecologist, was shot through the kitchen window of his Amherst home shortly after he and his wife returned home from the synagogue on Friday night, October 23.
He was one of three doctors who performed abortions in the Buffalo area.
Investigators in Canada and the United States are also investigating four other sniper attacks on doctors performing abortions in Canada and western New York.
Moskal said the two men apparently showed up at Slepian's house the day after his death.
They had tried to attend a prayer vigil for Slepian but were accidentally sent to his house and a police officer on duty took their names, Moskal said.
"They were wanted for interviews just because they were literally in the area after the murder," he said.
FBI officials said they were not sure whether Stauber or Gingrich were involved in anti-abortion activities.
The two men are believed to live in the Cleveland area.
Inspector Keith MacCaskill of the Winnipeg Police Service, a Canadian member of a law enforcement task force investigating all five shootings, said he had never heard of the men.
Moskal stressed that no one has been named as a suspect in Slepian's death.
"The very fact that we are interviewing someone does not make them a subject or a suspect," he said.
INDEPENDENCE, Mo. _ The North Atlantic Treaty Organization embraced three of its former rivals, the Czech Republic, Hungary, and Poland, on Friday, formally ending Soviet domination of those nations that began after World War II and opening a new path for the military alliance.
In a personal and emotional ceremony at the Truman Presidential Library, Secretary of State Madeleine Albright watched as the foreign ministers of the three countries signed the alliance's accession documents, signed them themselves, and then held them aloft like a trophy.
Albright, who was born in Prague and fled shortly after the Communist takeover in 1948, made no secret of her delight at the accession of her homeland and its neighbors to the alliance after a six-year transatlantic diplomatic process led by the Clinton administration.
"To quote an old Central European phrase:" Hallelujah, "" she said.
In Poland, Hungary and the Czech Republic, the moment was marked by small public celebrations.
In Warsaw, Poland, after dark, when fireworks lit up the sky, the Polish flag and NATO flag were raised side by side near the Tomb of the Unknown Soldier in Pilsudski Square.
In Hungary, the flags of NATO members were raised in front of the Ministry of Defense.
And in Prague, Czech Republic, President Vaclav Havel said in a statement: "Never before have we been part of such a broad, solid and binding security alliance, which at the same time respects the sovereignty and will of our nation."
Enlarging NATO from 16 to 19 members was one of the government's most important foreign policy goals, one that arose out of a desire, advocates say, to cement the democratic gains made in the former Warsaw Pact countries after the collapse of communism a decade ago.
But policymakers also faced critics who argued that welcoming these three new countries would draw a new border across Europe and turn Russians against them.
Since Friday, the border between Western Europe and the former Soviet Union has shifted to Poland's eastern border.
In her speech in the small hall, where dignitaries from the three countries sat and the three foreign ministers flanked her on stage, Albright countered that NATO would now do for Eastern Europe what NATO had already done for Western Europe.
"We will do everything in our power to protect our diplomatic and military peoples around the world," she said.
Clinton met with his top advisers at the White House on Wednesday to assess the situation following the two bombings of the U.S. embassies in Kenya and Tanzania that killed more than 250 people and wounded more than 5,000, most of them Kenyans and Tanzanians.
Twelve Americans were killed in the bombing of the Kenyan embassy.
Clinton ordered an assessment of the cost of rebuilding embassies in Kenya and Tanzania, as well as the cost of the emergency teams deployed to the two countries.
He also called for a "list of priorities to improve the security of U.S. facilities around the world."
The State Department and the Office of Management and Budget will jointly make the assessments "in the coming days," and the president will submit the report to Congress for further emergency funding.
The director of the US Federal Bureau of Investigation (FBI) Louis Freeh said here on Friday that the US airstrikes on Afghanistan and Sudan are not directly related to the investigation into the 7 August bombings in East Africa.
"This is an investigation on many fronts," Freeh said at a news conference at Jomo Kenyatta airport before returning home.
"I cannot detail or characterize the kind of evidence we gathered in Nairobi."
He said he could not confirm that there was evidence of a link between the bombings of the US embassies in Kenya and Tanzania and the Saudi millionaire and alleged main sponsor of anti-US terrorism, Osama Bin Laden, who now lives in Afghanistan.
"Hundreds of people have been questioned" and investigators would probably have to take quite a long time to gather evidence about the two bombings to reach any conclusion, he said.
Freeh flew to Washington immediately after the news conference.
US President Bill Clinton said Thursday that he ordered the attacks after receiving "compelling evidence" that US-raided terrorist facilities in Sudan and Afghanistan played a key role in the bombings in the capitals of Kenya and Tanzania.
However, Freeh said that although FBI, Kenyan and Tanzanian bomb investigators had worked very hard and made "very good progress" in the case, no conclusion or decision could be reached at this stage.
After a visit to the Tanzanian capital Dar es Salaam on Thursday and a meeting with Kenyan police chiefs on Friday morning, the FBI chief also said that he was very pleased with the close and effective cooperation between FBI agents and police in Kenya and Tanzania.
The man who hurled a grenade at U.S. Embassy guards seconds before the bomb exploded was identified on Thursday as two other suspects - an Arab and a Sudanese - who had been arrested, Kenyan newspapers reported Friday.
Local sources said the plan to bomb U.S. embassies in Kenya and Tanzania took three months and bombers destined for Kenya were sent through Somalia and Rwanda.
A senior government official who saw the confessions of the suspects was quoted by local media on Friday as saying that an Egyptian Islamic Jihad group linked to bin Laden had planned the attacks.
The car bomb attack on the US embassy in Nairobi killed 253 people and injured 5,000 others.
Ten people were killed and about 70 others injured in an almost simultaneous car bomb attack outside the U.S. Embassy in Tanzania.
Previously, the Taliban government had rejected the U.S. demand for Laden's extradition from Afghanistan.
The ratification process of NATO enlargement was completed on Wednesday after the Netherlands gave the green light.
Poland, Hungary and the Czech Republic were admitted as members of the Western military alliance after the upper house of the Dutch parliament voted in favour of their admission.
All parties in the upper house, with the exception of the Greens, agreed to the enlargement process, as reported here.
The House of Commons approved the expansion at the beginning of October this year.
The ratification process began in February this year, when Canada became the first NATO member state to begin the ratification process.
This was followed by Denmark, Norway, Germany, France, Greece, Luxembourg, Spain, the United Kingdom, the United States, Iceland, Belgium, Italy, Portugal and Turkey.
At the Madrid Summit last year, NATO decided to invite the three Eastern European countries to start accession talks.
Poland, Hungary and the Czech Republic are to become full members of NATO in April next year, when the alliance celebrates its 50th anniversary in Washington.
The North Atlantic Treaty Organization (NATO) decided in early March to admit Poland, Hungary and the Czech Republic, a representative of the organization said Wednesday.
The official said the accession was planned for early March, but an exact date has not yet been set.
He added that a formal accession would take place at the NATO summit in Washington on 24 and 25 April.
The accession in March will allow the three countries to sign the summit documents, which will include the new strategic concept of the alliance and the post-Madrid development plan, the official added.
The Western military alliance invited the three Central and Eastern European countries to join the 16-nation bloc at the Madrid summit in July 1997.
Following the ratification of the Accession Treaty by the Hungarian Parliament on Tuesday, the three countries have completed all the necessary formalities for accession.
Romania, Slovenia, Latvia, Estonia, Lithuania and Bulgaria are also candidates for NATO membership.
The Presidents of Poland and the Czech Republic signed accession papers to become members of the North Atlantic Treaty Organization (NATO) in simultaneous ceremonies broadcast live on Polish television in Warsaw and Prague.
Polish Presidents Aleksander Kwasniewski and Vaclav Havel signed the preparatory documents in their presidential palaces.
Polish Defense Minister Janusz Onyszkiewicz and other senior military officers also attended the ceremony.
Poland, the Czech Republic and Hungary, which were invited to join NATO in Madrid in July 1997, will officially join NATO on 12 March at a ceremony in Independence, Missouri.
The Hungarian Head of State, Arpad Goencz, signed Budapest's accession document on 10 February.
US Secretary of State Madeleine Albright has invited Polish Foreign Minister Bronisaw Geremek to visit the United States.
In her speech in the small hall, where dignitaries from the three countries sat and the three foreign ministers flanked her on stage, Albright countered that NATO would now do for Eastern Europe what NATO had already done for Western Europe.
We will steadily and systematically erase the line drawn in Europe by Stalin's bloody boots, without replacing it. "
Albright also used her speech to articulate a forward-looking vision for NATO, which celebrates its 50th anniversary next month in Washington, and to defend NATO's potential engagement in Kosovo.
After the Cold War, NATO must be ready to confront an aggressive regime, a rampaging splinter group, or a terrorist group.
And we know that if the past is prologue, we face a future in which weapons will be more destructive over longer distances than ever before. "
The NATO summit will produce an initiative that "responds to the serious threat posed by weapons of mass destruction and their delivery systems."
In Kosovo, where Yugoslav leader Slobodan Milosevic opposes the deployment of NATO-led troops, including 4,000 US troops, Albright insisted that NATO was the best tool to enforce a peace settlement.
"We need to make it clear that a solution without NATO-led enforcement is not acceptable because only NATO has the credibility and ability to make it work," she said.
"And we must resolutely explain the consequences of intransigence," she added, referring to the threat of NATO airstrikes against Milosevic if he does not agree to the deployment.
But in many ways, the day belonged to the Czech Republic, Hungary, and Poland, which, in order to meet NATO standards, had to downsize their Soviet-style armed forces, organize civilian control of their armies, and agree to buy NATO-compatible equipment in the coming years.
It was the spiritual homecoming to the West that all three foreign ministers emphasized.
"Poland is returning forever to where it always belonged: to the free world," said Polish Foreign Minister Bronislaw Geremek, who was a dissident during the communist era.
Geremek said he brought some appropriate memorabilia from Poland to the Truman Library, including a campaign poster from 1989, when the anti-communist Solidarity forces won against the communists.
The poster showed a picture of Gary Cooper from the movie "High Noon."
"It helped us win," Geremek said.
"Today is noon for the people of Poland." "
Czech Foreign Minister Jan Kavan, who lived in London from 1968, when the Soviets invaded Czechoslovakia, until 1989, said he remained animated during his opposition work as an emigrant because he believed that communism would eventually fall.
But he never dreamed that his country would become a member of NATO.
"Joining NATO is a guarantee that we will never again become impotent victims of foreign aggression," Kavan said.
Hungarian Foreign Minister Janos Martonyi, who remembers the 1956 uprising as a 13-year-old, also said that NATO membership meant that Hungary was "returning to its natural habitat."
"It was our obvious purpose to reunite those with whom we share the same values, interests and goals," he said.
But Martonyi also stressed that Hungary understands that NATO membership entails not only privileges but also obligations.
"We will prove that new members can actually increase the weight of the alliance," he said.
The venue for Friday's ceremony was chosen by Albright, who as secretary of state is the custodian of NATO accession agreements.
The secretary made the rather unorthodox decision to choose a location outside Washington because she wanted the ceremony to be aligned with the memories of President Truman, under whom NATO was founded.
On April 4, 1949, Truman's Secretary of State Dean Acheson signed, on behalf of the United States, the Washington Treaty that created NATO.
Acheson was Albright's favorite predecessor, her aides said.
Founded in response to the threat posed by the Soviet Union under Stalin, NATO calls for the collective defense of its members and was originally created with 12 members.
The alliance has already expanded three times, to include Greece and Turkey in 1952, West Germany in 1955, and Spain in 1982.
But NATO has never reached out to its former enemies in the Eastern Bloc.
The Clinton administration began expanding NATO in 1993 after Clinton met in Washington with President Havel and then-Polish President Lech Walesa.
Clinton's foreign policy team remembers these talks because they took place after the opening of the Holocaust Museum, and because they involved two very different Central European leaders delivering the same message.
US Secretary of State Madeleine Albright has invited Polish Foreign Minister Bronisaw Geremek to visit the United States.
He will then travel to Washington DC on March 12 to submit the document.
Poland, Hungary and the Czech Republic officially joined NATO on Friday at a ceremony in the city of Independence, Missouri.
At the Harry Truman Library in Independence, the three foreign ministers Bronislaw Geremek (Poland), Janos Martonyi (Hungary) and Jan Kavan (Czech Republic) signed formal accession documents.
In speeches before the signing ceremony, ministers said that their countries saw NATO membership as a guarantee of security and recognition of their rightful place in Europe.
Kavan said the Czech Republic would no longer be "the powerless victim of a foreign invasion."
Martonyi called Hunragy's admission to NATO a "return to her natural habitat" and said, "It was our obvious destiny to join those with whom we share the same values, interests, and goals."
And Geremek spoke of a "great day for Poland and for the world."
Hungary's accession to NATO has changed the political balance in Central and Eastern Europe and shaken the world order since World War II, Hungarian Prime Minister Viktor Orban said here on Friday.
Speaking in front of the Dutch Embassy, where the NATO flag was raised, Orban said the occasion was the end of the embassy's liaison mission between Hungary and NATO (North Atlantic Treaty Organization).
The Hungarian government held a series of celebrations on Friday to mark the country's accession to NATO.
In a televised address on Friday, President Arpad Gencz called the accession a "historic event" that demonstrated the credibility and realistic approach of the country's foreign policy.
Poland and the Czech Republic were among the first three former Warsaw Pact countries to join NATO on Friday.
The ambassadors of Hungary, Poland and the Czech Republic issued a joint statement on Friday saying that the accession of the three countries to NATO will strengthen Europe's security and help eliminate threats to Europe.
Five of the country's major political parties issued statements in support of the country's NATO membership.
But some other parties and social organizations oppose NATO enlargement, claiming that Hungary's accession to NATO would damage the country's independence and bring new problems and economic burdens.
Poland, Hungary and the Czech Republic officially joined NATO on Friday at a ceremony in the city of Independence, Missouri.
At the Harry Truman Library in Independence, the three foreign ministers Bronislaw Geremek (Poland), Janos Martonyi (Hungary) and Jan Kavan (Czech Republic) signed formal accession documents.
In speeches before the signing ceremony, ministers said that their countries saw NATO membership as a guarantee of security and recognition of their rightful place in Europe.
Kavan said the Czech Republic would no longer be "the powerless victim of a foreign invasion."
Martonyi called Hunragy's admission to NATO a "return to her natural habitat" and said, "It was our obvious destiny to join those with whom we share the same values, interests, and goals."
And Geremek spoke of a "great day for Poland and for the world."
US Secretary of State Madeleine Albright accepted the admission papers of the foreign ministers of the three countries, marking their formal accession to the 50-year-old Western alliance.
"Hallelujah," cried Albright, a daughter of Czechoslovakia who fled her homeland as a child and later became a U.S. citizen.
Ahead of the ceremony, NATO Secretary General Javier Solana said that the accession of the three countries "is perhaps the clearest evidence that Europe is growing closer together."
"Extending membership to these three democracies will help stabilise a region that has historically been the scene of many disasters this century," Solana added, referring to the two world wars.
The State Department said the three new members "enhance NATO's ability to fulfill its core mission of collective defense, respond to a range of security challenges, and reduce the possibility of another major conflict in Europe that has claimed so many American lives."
Their message was that NATO should be used as a mechanism to ensure their countries "democratic progress while dissuading Russia from further imperial intentions.
The U.S. ambassador to Poland, Dan Fried, who was on the National Security Council at the time, said Friday that he remembered being asked to "translate that pious desire into policy."
In July 1997, the Alliance announced at a summit in Madrid that it would welcome Poland, Hungary and the Czech Republic.
Romania and Slovenia, which had tried to meet the standards, were rejected, at least for the time being.
One problem was dealing with the Russians, who oscillated between open hostility and acceptance of the seemingly inevitable.
In early 1997, President Boris Yeltsin told President Clinton that he could live with NATO enlargement.
But, to accommodate the Russians, NATO created the NATO-Russia Council as an advisory body that formalized relations between Moscow and the Alliance.
Poland reacted sensitively to Russia's reaction to NATO membership, and Geremek did everything he could to reassure the Russians.
"We want our NATO membership to serve as a catalyst for Polish-Russian cooperation," he said last year.
Still, the ministers on the secretary of state's plane that brought Albright and the three foreign ministers to Friday's ceremony were asked if they could ever imagine Russia as a NATO member.
Then Martonyi replied: "Silence is your answer."
With the discovery of a buried rifle 11 days ago, authorities in the Buffalo, New York area say they have gathered enough evidence for the October killing of Dr. Barnett Slepian to begin presenting a murder case to a state grand jury as early as next week.
A federal grand jury has already considered the killing under laws that prohibit violence against abortion providers.
Slepian was the mainstay of Buffalo's only abortion clinic before he was shot dead at his home in suburban Amherst on October 23 last year.
But District Attorney Frank C. Clark's decision to begin taking evidence before a state grand jury suggests he has amassed enough material to support a murder charge.
"We're at a point where we know what we want to present to a grand jury," Clark said Monday in a telephone interview.
Although authorities have not said who the suspect is, all attention so far has been focused on James C. Kopp, a 44-year-old anti-abortion activist who has traveled from demonstration to demonstration across the country for years.
Officially, Kopp is wanted as a "material witness" in the case.
Clark said it would be easier to request the extradition of an accused suspect from another state or country than from someone wanted as a material witness.
For now, however, that would be a theoretical advantage, since the authorities have admitted that they have no idea where Kopp is.
Clark said there was "no question" that the discovery of the gun was important in his decision to press charges.
The rifle was discovered a metre underground on April 8, about 200 feet behind Slepian's home.
It is currently being tested at the FBI lab in Washington, but authorities have said it resembles the type of high-powered rifle used in the killing.
Clark said other evidence prosecutors could present included a strand of human hair found at the wooded shooting site that "highly likely" matched Kopps' hair, as well as testimony from a jogger who discovered Kopps' car near Slepian's home 10 days before the 6 a.m. shooting.
There are also videotapes of Kopps' car crossing the Canadian border in 1994, 1995 and 1997, when three Canadian doctors performing abortions were shot at.
None of these doctors were killed.
If a suspect is apprehended and charged in both federal and state courts, federal proceedings would likely be preceded by federal proceedings, giving authorities two chances to prosecute.
New York State's double jeopardy laws would make it more difficult to prosecute a suspect found not guilty in a federal case.
U.S. Attorney Denise E. O'Donnell declined to comment on the federal charges, but she said that in a case like this, potential charges of abortion-related violence, the use of a firearm in an act of violence, crossing state lines to commit a crime, and, if the suspect's act was linked to an organization, would be violations of the so-called RICO statutes, which prohibit an organized criminal enterprise.
The 52-year-old Slepian, a father of four boys, was shot at 10 p.m. while chatting with his wife in the kitchen of their home in an affluent enclave in Amherst, the largest suburb of Buffalo.
US law enforcement officials said on Wednesday they had concluded that James Kopp, a passionate and nomadic anti-abortion activist, fired the shots that killed a doctor at an abortion clinic in Buffalo, New York, last October.
Based on physical evidence - including a rifle found near the doctor's home in April and a match between fibers found near the murder and others found in Kopp's car - officials said they had decided to issue an arrest warrant for Dr. Barnett Slepian.
Kopp, a fugitive, has so far been described only as a factual witness and a federal arrest warrant has been issued to arrest him for questioning.
There was no official confirmation of the decision to update the warrant, but Denise O'Donnell, the U.S. Attorney for the Western District of New York in Buffalo, has scheduled a news conference for Thursday to announce a "significant development" in the investigation into Slepian's murder.
Erie County District Attorney Frank Clark, Bernard Tolbert, the FBI special agent in charge of the Buffalo office, and other investigators will also be at the scene.
Because there is no federal homicide law as such, federal officials said Kopp could be charged under the recently passed Free Access to Hospital Entrances Act, which provides for a sentence of up to life in prison for someone convicted of physical assault or threats against abortion providers.
If Kopp is also charged and convicted of murder, he could face the death penalty.
Slepian, a 52-year-old obstetrician and gynecologist who was the mainstay of Buffalo's only abortion clinic, was shot dead on the night of October 23 as he stood in the kitchen of his home in suburban Amherst, New York, chatting with his wife and one of his four sons.
No weapon was found in the initial police searches around Slepian's home.
But on April 8, investigators, wondering how the attacker could have come out of the woods without fear of being seen with a rifle, searched the scene of the shooting again and discovered a rifle buried a foot underground 200 feet behind Slepian's house.
Clark, the Erie County district attorney, said afterward that investigators believed the doctor's killer buried the gun in the days before the shooting, dug up the gun and fired the fatal shots, then hastily buried it again and fled.
Kopp, 44, who appears gaunt and bearded in FBI photos and whose last known addresses were in Vermont and New Jersey, became the focus of the investigation within two weeks of the killing.
A jogger observed Kopp's car near Slepian's home at 6 a.m. 10 days before the murder and noted down the license plate out of curiosity as to why a stranger had parked there so early.
A six-year-old boy who came to the U.S. coast as a motherless refugee and became a political symbol for anti-Castro exiles must be returned to his father in Cuba, immigration authorities ruled Wednesday.
Faced with the choice of accommodating Elian Gonzalez with relatives in Miami, where the boy was wooed with American toys and theme park visits, or returning him to his apparently distraught father in Havana, the Immigration and Naturalization Department decided that the boy should go home by January 14, INS Commissioner Doris Meissner told reporters.
"This little boy, who has been through so much, belongs to his father," Meissner said at a news conference.
The decision was met with outrage by Cuban Americans, who said the boy would suffer under Fidel Castro's repressive communist regime.
Lawyers for the boy's family in Miami sent protest letters to Attorney General Janet Reno and President Clinton, saying they were ready to appeal if Reno and Clinton despise the Gonzalez family.
Anti-Castro protesters crowded the street near the local INS office in Miami on Wednesday, demanding that Elian be allowed to stay.
"Today is a sad day for the U.S. Constitution... and for the hopes and dreams of Elian's mother Elizabeth," who died along with nine other adults when her boat sank while trying to reach Florida, said Roger Bernstein, who represented the Miami Gonzalez family.
"Nobody wants the boy to be separated from his father," said Ninoska Perez of the Miami-based Cuban-American National Foundation.
'But this little boy has earned the right to be free here by surviving two days on a tube, 'he said.
In Havana, the government reacted cautiously to the INS ruling, warning against "excessive optimism" and predicting that "the Cuban-American mafia and the extreme right in the US Congress" would still fight to keep the boy.
Meissner said the INS decision was made after two long conversations with the boy's father, Juan Miguel Gonzalez, a hotel employee in Cardenas, Cuba, who Meissner said was eager to bring Elian back to him.
Since the boy was so young, Meißner said, the biological father had the right to speak for Elian.
There was "no information" that González, who was divorced from Elian's mother, was an unfit father or that he had been coerced by the Castro regime to campaign for his son's return, Meissner said.
Spencer Eig, a lawyer for the Gonzalez family in Miami, said Meissner's assumptions were "unconstitutional and untrue."
"Elian cannot apply for asylum because of the persecution by Fidel Castro's government," Eig complained.
'If only his father, who is under the control of the Fidel Castro regime, could do it for him... that's deeply unfair and untrue. '
Meissner gave no reason for the January 14 deadline, and it is still unclear how Elian will be returned to Cuba if the González family's appeals are unsuccessful.
The INS offered three options: for Gonzalez to pick up his son, for the family in Miami to take him to Cuba, or for a "third party" to accompany the child to his home country.
Gonzalez had indicated - possibly under pressure from the Castro regime - that he did not want to travel to Miami, and there was "no possibility" that the Gonzalez would send Elian back to Cuba from Miami, Perez said.
A third companion, Elian, frees the INS from the politically uncomfortable spectacle of having to do the job itself.
The Cuban-American lobby is a locally powerful political force that few politicians want to provoke.
Clinton has tried to distance herself from the controversy from the beginning.
"I told you when we started, I will do my best to keep the decision out of politics," Clinton told reporters in the Rose Garden on Wednesday.
But if Elian is forced to return to Cuba, the Clinton-Gore administration, especially (Vice President) Al Gore, will pay a heavy price in terms of votes in Florida, "Perez said.
Cuba experts and custody lawyers said the INS ruling was appropriate under the law.
"It's high time" the boy was sent back to his birthplace, said Wayne Smith, a former Clinton administration adviser on Cuba.
"Every federal court and family court in the United States would award custody to a surviving parent, not a distant relative who leads a more prosperous life," said David Abraham, a professor at Miami Law School and an expert on custody law.
"Maybe we have Disneylands and Universal Studios and all those things dangling in front of the little boy's eyes," Abraham added.
Given the political ramifications, the Elian Gonzalez case is unlikely to be concluded quickly, said Richard Nuccio, a former Cuba adviser to the Clinton administration.
"Since two congressmen represent the district of Elian's family, I don't see how Elian could be sent back by the federal government without going through the court system," Nuccio said.
The New York Times wrote in an editorial on Thursday, January 6:
The U.S. Immigration and Customs Enforcement made a sensible decision Wednesday when it ruled that Elian Gonzalez, the 6-year-old boy who survived an ill-fated smuggling trip across the Florida Strait, should be reunited with his father in Cuba.
The decision will shake up anti-Castro Cubans in Miami, but INS commissioner Doris Meissner made it clear that the service had followed established policy in the case.
In addition, by setting a return date of January 14, the Immigration Department has given Elian's American relatives time to review the ruling in court.
Elian was one of 13 Cubans who tried to get from Cuba to Florida in a 17-foot aluminum motorboat six weeks ago when it capsized.
Ten people on board died, including his mother and stepfather.
The boy clung to a hose for two days and was pulled from the Atlantic Ocean and brought ashore on Thanksgiving Day.
He was immediately released into the care of relatives in Miami, and just as quickly embraced as a pawn in the toxic estrangement between Fidel Castro's regime and his exiled opponents.
This side has argued from the outset that Elian should not be denied due process because of his youth, and the INS seems to have moved cautiously.
Elian's great-uncle and other American relatives were allowed to live in Miami.
The boy's divorced father, Juan Miguel Gonzalez, was also interviewed and confirmed to the agency that he had a close and committed relationship with his son.
The mother's parents also spoke out in favor of returning to Cuba.
The INS ruled that González passed the test, which in such cases requires the return of custody to the sole surviving parent, as part of its prevailing policy.
"This little boy who has been through so much belongs to his father," Meissner said Wednesday, adding, "The unique relationship between parent and child and family reunification has long been a cornerstone of both American immigration law and INS practice."
The White House immediately announced support for the decision.
It is unclear how the political side of the affair will play out.
Elian's relatives in Miami have said they will appeal, which is their right.
Some of their more militant supporters in Miami, who dislike the prospect of the boy being used as a "trophy" by Castro, have hinted that they might try to disrupt efforts to reunite Elian with his father.
That would mean treating the boy like a trophy in a diplomatic battle that took place decades before he was born.
Good for Attorney General Janet Reno for the reasonable opinion she gave in the Elian Gonzalez case.
Some semblance of constitutional order was needed after a Miami judge turned legal logic on its head to refer his relatives in Florida's appeal to keep the six-year-old in the United States to their family court.
If the Immigration and Naturalization Department's order that Elian be returned to his father in Cuba is to be challenged, Reno has correctly ruled that the only appropriate jurisdiction is the federal court, not the state or municipality.
If the relatives appeal to the Federal Court, the chances of a positive verdict are slim.
For starters, the INS has already taken the position that the only person who can ask for US asylum for Elian is his father, which he will not do.
Furthermore, immigration law stipulates that asylum can only be granted if there is an immediate threat of torture or persecution in the applicant's home country, which no one expects in Elian's case.
It is not enough to argue that Elian could suffer deprivation or oppression in Fidel Castro's failed communist state.
The INS routinely sends potential immigrants back to countries where poverty and tyranny are worse than in Cuba.
Most importantly, after losing his mother and stepfather in a catastrophic sea voyage to the United States last November, Elian needs the love and care of surviving family members who have known him all his life - his father and both grandparents in Cuba.
They may not give him all the material things his benefactors in Miami can, but there are intangibles in close family ties that are more valuable than bicycles and trips to Disney World.
The next item on the agenda for the Justice Department and the INS is to resolve Elian's case immediately.
They must not tolerate delays by lawyers for his relatives in Miami.
Moreover, the longer his return is delayed, the greater the likelihood of meddling by calculating members of Congress playing into the hands of the anti-Castro lobby.
A prominent Indiana Republican has already called on Elian to testify before a House committee, and others in the Senate are preparing to grant the boy US citizenship.
Both tricks are intended to thwart his reunion with his father.
The Justice Department and the INS must not allow Capitol Hill opportunists to cling to Elian.
The angry crowd stormed toward Manuel Gonzalez as he left the Miami courthouse, shaking his fists and shouting "communist" and "traitor" in Spanish.
But the great uncle of the 6-year-old survivor of the shipwreck, rafter Elian González, just shook his head and walked faster.
Manuel González, 59, a quiet bus mechanic, has largely disappeared from the glare of television in a drama that has divided his once close family and could finally be resolved in a court hearing the week of March 6.
He found himself in the middle of the swirling González family saga when Elian's father, Juan Miguel, sent a letter to U.S. Attorney Janet Reno asking for his son to be moved to Manuel González's house.
Alone among his two brothers and two sisters who fled Cuba for Miami, Manuel Gonzalez believes Elian should return to Cuba to live with his father.
Reno rejected the request, but Manuel Gonzalez did not relent.
In a city where even the slightest endorsement of Cuban President Fidel Castro can trigger threats of physical violence, Manuel González filed a motion in federal court seeking custody of Elian so that he could help reunite the boy with his father in Cuba.
It was a move seen by many as bold and principled, but others fear it could be foolish.
"I lost a son," Gonzalez later told reporters, alluding to his own son's cancer death.
< p > I support Juan Miguel because I know what it means to lose a son.
His sincerity did not stop the chatter on the streets of Little Havana or on Miami's ubiquitous Spanish-language radio stations, where callers berated González for playing into the hands of a tyrant.
A day after being chased on the steps of the federal courthouse, Manuel Gonzalez did not come to the door of his modest home in Miami's Little Havana.
"He needs to go to work," said his daughter, Rosa, who said Gonzalez would prefer not to talk about the issue.
After weeks of high-profile sparring with allegations of abuse, spying, and betrayal flying back and forth across the Florida Straits, the chances that the Gonzalez family could ever heal the wounds inflicted by the Elian saga seem slim.
But the family has overcome steep obstacles before.
Long before Elian thrust them into the relentless glare of the international spotlight, the González clan was a typical example of the conflicts and painful divisions that the Cuban Revolution created.
Of the nine brothers and sisters of the Manuel generation, five have come to Miami, while three remain in Cuba and one has died.
According to press reports, Caridad González, the eldest sister, was the first to come to the United States in 1966.
Her brother Delfin, who was imprisoned as an anti-Castro activist in the 1960s, followed in 1979.
Then came another sister, Georgina, in 1983, followed by Manuel and Lazaro in 1984.
Of those who remained in Cuba, Juan Miguel's father, Juan Gonzalez, appears to be the Cuban government's closest ally.
He worked as a police officer in a Cuban ministry, a position that probably helped his son Elian's father get a job in the communist island's increasingly lucrative tourism industry.
The family's experience is similar to that of thousands of others now split between Miami and Cuba, said Damien Fernandez, a Cuban-American political scientist at Florida International University.
"Cubans value their families above almost anything else, but politics after 1959 has managed to divide thousands of families," he said.
For decades, Cuban-Americans in Miami were largely cut off from the families they left behind.
Labeled "worms" and "traitors" by Castro, many have been unable to speak to their relatives, let alone visit them, for years.
The situation eased in the early 1990s, when the crisis was triggered by the end of subsidies from the former Soviet Union.
Desperate for hard currency, Castro eased travel restrictions and allowed Cubans to own US dollars, triggering a flow of cash from Miami that soon became a flood.
By some estimates, the 700,000 Cubans living in South Florida and elsewhere in the United States send $1 billion a year in remittances to their families on the island.
"Families in Miami have become a lifeline, and there's been a big trend toward reconciliation since 1990," said Fernandez of Florida International.
"Although many families are still divided by politics, they have learned to forgive and forget and find common ground."
Branches of the family made regular phone calls, and relatives from Miami returned to Cuba for several family reunions.
But Elian's case has shattered the family's fragile truce, and their political differences have been exacerbated by inflammatory rhetoric between Castro's government and exiled extremists in Miami.
After expressing his opinion, Manuel Gonzalez was reportedly ostracized by his relatives in Miami, especially Lazaro, who kept Elian and went to court to try to keep the boy here.
The day before the federal court hearing last Tuesday, Manuel went to Lazaro's house to try to resolve their differences.
Neither side wanted to say what had happened, but when Manuel returned to his house, he was so upset that he felt palpitations and was taken to a hospital.
He was soon released and seemed reasonably healthy when he appeared in court the next morning.
But as exiled Cubans cheered Lazaro and his family outside the courthouse, their angry jabs seemed to shock Manuel.
But his lawyer insists he will continue to stand up for what he believes is right.
"He's a strong man," Jeffrey Leving said.
'He's not intimidated by anyone to do what's in Elian's best interests. '
To his credit, Lazaro Gonzalez issued a statement urging the exiles in Miami to respect his brother's views.
Manuel Gonzalez told reporters he believes his family will recover "at least in part" from the divisions that Elian's case has brought.
"The wounds of the problem will heal," he told the Los Angeles Times newspaper.
Elected officials in and around Miami warned U.S. immigration officials on Wednesday that they should expect little help from police when they arrive for 6-year-old Elian Gonzalez, and they said the city could erupt into violence if federal agents try to forcibly remove the boy from his great-uncle's home in Little Havana and send him back to Cuba.
"We will not provide our respective resources, whether in the form of police officers or other resources, to assist the federal government in any way, shape or form in bringing Elian Gonzalez back to Cuba inappropriately," Miami-Dade County Mayor Alex Penelas said.
As of Wednesday evening, Lazaro Gonzalez, the boy's great-uncle and provisional guardian, had still not signed an agreement to turn the child over to immigration authorities if relatives in Miami lose a fight to keep him when the case is heard in the 11th U.S. Circuit Court of Appeals in May.
"I'm not cooperating in anything," Gonzalez told a Spanish-language television station.
'The boy lives in my house and they've got to find him there. '
As pressure built in this city, home to a large number of exiled Cubans, immigration and naturalization officials said they did not plan to remove Elian on Thursday morning, even if his relatives in Miami continued to defy them.
However, an INS spokeswoman said the agency would revoke Elian's residency permit in the United States, clearing the way for his return to Cuba.
Cuban President Fidel Castro said in Havana that the boy's father, Juan Miguel Gonzalez, would travel to the United States to bring his son home following the court's decision on custody.
Elian's father was accompanied by other family members from Cuba, some of the boy's former classmates and teachers, as well as psychiatric experts, Castro said on Cuban television.
"We have the perfect formula to reinstate Elian," Castro said.
He also said the group would be willing to wait as long as it takes to bring the boy back.
A Justice Department spokesman declined to comment on Castro's announcement, saying:
Certainly, it changes things.
How it changes things is a little hard to say at this point.
If the father turns up in Miami and decides to ring his relatives and find his son, well, the family in Miami has put on record that they would hand Elian over to him.
Whether they actually do that remains to be seen. "
Even miles away from the waving protesters who stand guard outside the tiny house in Little Havana where Elian González lives with his great-uncle, they talk about the standoff between Elian's relatives in Miami and immigration and naturalization officials, who stream hot and fast from the coffee stands on Calle Ocho and every other place where Cuban Americans gather.
In fast-paced Spanish, so fast that even people who have learned the language in other Hispanic cultures struggle to keep up, they berate Attorney General Janet Reno, President Clinton, and others who want to send the child back to his father in Cuba.
And they question or mock the news that the boy's father is planning to come here to get his son.
It's a communist plot hatched by the hated dictator Fidel Castro, they say.
If you're not Cuban, they said, you just wouldn't understand.
"If it wasn't Cuba, the child would have to go to his father," said Delfina Miranda, 49, who works at a beauty salon in Little Havana.
This child will get nothing in Cuba.
Time and again, Cuban Americans said that outsiders who had not suffered under the Castro regime simply could not understand their passions in the standoff.
Talks between lawyers for Elian's relatives in Miami and government officials ended without result.
US immigration authorities have postponed until Tuesday morning any action to revoke the boy's temporary residence permit in the United States, which would allow federal officials to eventually remove the boy from his relatives' home and eventually return him to Cuba.
"Like the family, we are following developments regarding the possible arrival of (Elian's father) Juan Miguel Gonzalez in the United States," said Robert Wallis, INS director for Miami County.
Government officials had threatened to rescind the boy's parole if his great uncle Lazaro Gonzalez did not sign a document agreeing to extradite Elian to federal officials once the family's appeal to the 11th U.S. Circuit Court of Appeals is decided.
But the standoff became less significant in the eyes of Cuban Americans after Castro announced Wednesday that the boy's father, Juan Miguel Gonzalez, was willing to travel to the United States to take his son home.
Gonzalez's lawyer Greg Craig applied Thursday for a visa for his client for the expected visit, but was told by State Department officials that Gonzalez himself would have to apply for the visa at the U.S. diplomatic mission in Havana.
"Juan Miguel Gonzalez is ready to come to the United States immediately," Craig said.
But in Miami, many Cuban Americans said Castro staged the visit.
Castro said Gonzalez would travel with his current wife and son, a senior government official, doctors, nurses, psychiatrists, Elian's Cuban kindergarten teacher, classmates and his old school desk. Ricardo Alarcon, president of the Cuban National Assembly, was also on a list of 31 people released by the Cuban government on Thursday.
Castro has suggested that Elian and his relatives stay at the residence of Cuba's chief diplomat in Washington.
Hidden in the entourage, Cuban Americans here warned, Cuban security officials will make sure Gonzalez and everyone else make no mistake.
"They come with everyone, including psychologists," said Armando Sanjurjo, 73.
< p > Do you know why?
To give Elian an an injection and bring him back to Cuba. "
Sipping from a cafecito in front of the Versailles restaurant, the nerve center of Cuban political debate here in Little Havana, Sanjurjo, like many here, is convinced that the entourage is designed to prevent defections.

Throughout the week, elected officials in Washington, D.C., and in the state capital of Tallahassee have issued statements of support for Elian's relatives in Miami, even Vice President Al Gore breaking with his own administration's position.
In Miami-Dade County, elected officials went so far as to say their police departments would play no role in getting the boy out of the house.

Faced with the tense wait in Miami, immigration authorities announced Monday that they will instruct relatives of 6-year-old Cuban rafter Elian Gonzalez in Miami to hand the boy over to his father, who could come to the United States from Cuba as early as Tuesday.
"Our goal is to reunite Elian and his father," said Maria Cardona, a spokeswoman for the U.S. Immigration and Naturalization Service.
"The question is not whether we hand Elian over to his father, but when and how."
Late Monday, State Department officials announced that travel visas had been approved for Elian's father, Juan Miguel Gonzalez, his immediate family and three others - a pediatrician, a psychologist and one of Elian's favorite cousins.
According to unconfirmed media reports, the group could arrive in the US as early as Tuesday.
But Miami's fugitive Cuban exiles continued to vow to block any attempt to bring Elian home from tiny Havana, where he lives with relatives in Miami, with some predicting bloodshed if federal agents arrived for the boy.
"There will be thousands here to stop him when they come for him," said Ana Bonnin, one of about 100 exiles who practiced forming a human chain outside the house on Monday.

You can't control everyone. ""
Late in the day, lawyers for Elian's family arrived in Miami from their third one-day meeting with federal officials, but said little.
"We remain very concerned about the mental and physical well-being of this six-year-old," said Manny Diaz, one of the family's lawyers.
"We will be back tomorrow to continue our negotiations," he said.
It was not clear if the Gonzalez family would come to Miami to pick up Elian, or if they would travel to Washington, D.C. instead.
Cuban President Fidel Castro has said they will live in the house of a Cuban diplomat in the Washington area until the court case over Elian's fate is decided.
Visas for the entourage of more than 20 teachers, classmates and others - including Ricardo Alarcon, president of the Cuban National Assembly - whom Castro plans to accompany with his family have not yet been approved, officials said.
The flurry of events ends a four-month ordeal that has catapulted Elian into the center of an international tug of war.
He was found floating in a hose off the Florida coast on Thanksgiving Day after a boat with him on board sank off Cuba.
Federal agents placed him with relatives in Miami but kept him in custody.
In January, Immigration and Naturalization Commissioner Doris Meissner ruled that Elian should return to Cuba, a decision that was upheld by U.S. Attorney Janet Reno.
Relatives in Miami filed a federal lawsuit to overturn the decision, but a federal judge in Miami ruled against them in March.
They immediately appealed to the 11th Circuit Court of Appeals in Atlanta.
Legal experts and federal officials say Reno could allow the boy to be sent back to Cuba before the appeal is heard.
Last week, federal officials wanted the family to sign an agreement to voluntarily extradite the boy if they lose their appeal.
If the family refused, INS had threatened to rescind Elian's parole, meaning federal agents would remove him from the family in Miami.
The family refused to sign the agreement after two days of negotiations, and INS extended its deadline until 9am EST on Tuesday.
But the terms of the talks changed dramatically when Castro announced last Wednesday that the boy's father was ready to travel to the United States.
Earlier, Gonzalez had said he would never come here to get his son.
Over the weekend, lawyers for the family made allegations that Juan Miguel Gonzalez abused his son in phone calls, saying he told the boy that his mother was alive and waiting for him in Cuba.
But on Monday, federal officials hardened their bargaining position, insisting that the boy would be handed over to his father once the Cuban group arrives in the United States.
Efforts to keep Elian in this country through federal legislation appear to be thwarted, as most lawmakers seem to agree that the boy should return to Cuba.
A new ABC News / Washington Post poll shows that 6 in 10 Americans agree that the boy should go home with his father.
But in Miami, exiled Cubans insist that the majority of Americans do not understand the dynamics of life in Cuba.
How far they are prepared to go to prove their point remains to be seen.
"When he comes back, he won't be with his real father," said Elias Hernandez, who mingled with the crowd outside the home of Elian's relatives in Miami.
The New York Times wrote in an editorial on Thursday, April 6:
Juan Miguel Gonzalez made the right decision to travel to the United States on Thursday to reaffirm his desire to be reunited with his son Elian.
His presence on American soil is necessary to dispel doubts about his suitability as a father raised maliciously by Elian's relatives in Miami, and to end speculation that he is indifferent to reclaiming custody of his son.
Since González is virtually out of sight in Cuba, relatives have been free to distort the case with bogus legal arguments and unsubstantiated claims that Elian is afraid to see his father.
As soon as González comes to the United States, it would be better if he went to Miami instead of Washington. Only Castro's staunchest enemies can argue that Elian should not be reunited with him.
Gonzalez's arrival should also help clear the way for the Justice Department to make arrangements to transfer custody of the child.
Relatives in Miami have left open whether they will voluntarily surrender custody of Elian, and have done their best to stoke emotions against the Justice Department among Cuban-Americans in South Florida.
Hoping to trump the law, they have unreasonably demanded that a panel of child psychologists decide Elian's fate.
The best outcome would be for the relatives to hand Elian over to his father as soon as González arrives, in a spirit of kinship that makes Elian's transition as smooth as possible.
Once that happens, González and Elian, though technically free to return to Cuba, should remain here to await a decision by the appeals court on the case, which is expected next month.
But if the relatives balk, the Justice Department should quickly strip them of custody of the child and seek a federal court order ordering them and their lawyers to extradite Elian to his father.
Relatives may be less defiant when they are brought to justice.
If a court order does not enforce compliance, the Ministry of Justice will have to consider stricter measures, because relatives will then oppose the rule of law as defined by both the ministry and the courts.
But Washington should not rush to forcibly remove Elian from relatives.
This course would psychologically hurt the boy and could lead to a violent confrontation with demonstrators who gather daily in front of the relatives "house.
In this case, the government is not relinquishing its legal or moral authority by giving relatives and supporters time to reflect and seek a softer solution.
For reasons of law, compassion, and common sense, the government has the right to reunite Elian with his father, and it has the power to enforce that right, despite the bluster of Miami's demagogic political leaders.
But for now, all parties should seek an amicable end.
Juan González does his part by making the short journey across the Florida Strait to erase any question about his love for his son.
There are no Cuban flags flying at his door, and no political signs urging the fall of the young Elian González hang on his windows.
And while keeping her distance from the heated rhetoric and politics that envelop Miami, she had a clear view on Friday on the case of the 6-year-old Cuban boy who has ruled the city for nearly five months.
"I feel for this child," Juanita Castro said.
In both Cuba and Miami, this is too politicized, and too many people are benefiting from this tragedy.
I think people have forgotten that this is a human issue. ""
Juanita Castro, who arrived in Miami in 1961, believes custody of the child should be settled between family members.
She is not sure whether the boy should stay in the United States, but she believes that since he has been here so long, he should be given the opportunity to live in a free country.
Asked if she believed the boy's father, Juan Miguel Gonzalez, was controlled by the Cuban government even though he had been in Bethesda, Md., for a week, she frowned.
"I don't know what Juan Miguel thinks or believes," Juanita Castro said.
"What I think is that he should have arrived in this country at least 72 hours after his son was found floating off the coast of Fort Lauderdale," he said.

And now it's become a tragedy. '

Juanita Castro said she has not visited Elian Gonzalez's relatives in Miami and has no plans to do so.

"I just work every day in my pharmacy and pray for a solution in our country (Cuba) that doesn't involve violence," she said.
And I pray every day for this little boy because he needs help.
The Clinton administration's wisdom in returning Elian González to his father was confirmed over the weekend by the decision of his relatives in Miami and their Republican allies to further politicize what was supposed to be a simple child custody case.
Elian finally gets time to reconnect with his father in a quiet, neutral environment, where he is no longer a metaphor for the war between the Cubans.
A few miles from his temporary home at Andrews Air Force Base, Senator Bob Smith of New Hampshire, Representative Tom DeLay of Texas, both Republicans, and relatives treated the raid to bring Elian back as if it were a Nazi SS operation.
Still, federal agents had an arrest warrant, enforced U.S. law, and entered the house with the necessary force to ensure minimal resistance and injury.
The raid was necessary because Gonzalez's relatives in Miami, who refused to return the boy, made him a symbol of resistance to Fidel Castro.
Elian printed his name on an asylum application and was put in front of a video camera to say he did not want to return to Cuba.
Relatives treated the boy as a political prop.
If the relatives had allowed Elian to return to Cuba, they would have denied the dictator the opportunity to revive support for his regime.
Juan Miguel González is apparently a Castro supporter, but that should not stop him from being reunited with his child.
Ever since he first learned that Elian was in Florida last November, he has been working tirelessly to get the boy back.
Elian has developed a great affection for relatives in Miami.
It would be ideal if they could have returned him to his father but kept in touch while their appeal was pending in court.
Negotiations broke down, however, because they insisted on taking the father's place instead of being treated as concerned but distant relatives.
The father can stay here, but he can also return to Cuba if he wants.
Like any other 6-year-old in the care of a loving parent, Elian should go where that parent wants.
Elian Gonzalez will remain in the United States for at least another two weeks, even after a federal appeals court on Thursday declined to order a hearing on political asylum for the embattled Cuban child.
Elian could be forced to stay longer if relatives in Miami, who are seeking asylum for the child because of his Cuban father's objections, appeal further.
Attorney Kendall Coffey said his clients would "seriously consider" an appeal to the Supreme Court.
Relatives in Miami could also request a rehearing before the same three-judge panel that issued Thursday's ruling, or the entire 11th U.S. Circuit Court of Appeals.
But Thursday's ruling made clear that the family has only 14 days to decide whether to request a rehearing at that level.
& LR; < p > Don't expect any extensions, "the court said.
The ruling follows six months of international family disputes, political recriminations and widespread protests in Miami and Cuba over the fate of the six-year-old boy.
His mother died trying to smuggle Elian out of Cuba, and it was difficult for family members, Miami community leaders, and many politicians to separate attitudes toward Cuban leader Fidel Castro from the related legal issues.
The appeal ruling made clear that the court has no pleasure in removing an obstacle to Elian's return to Cuba.
"We recognize as a widely accepted truth that Cuba violates human rights and fundamental freedoms and does not guarantee the rule of law to people living in Cuba," Judge J.L. Edmonson wrote on behalf of the panel.
But the Immigration and Naturalization Department's decision to deny Elian an an asylum hearing was "within the bounds of reasonable decisions," the statement said.
"The court approves or disapproves of the INS decision to reject the asylum applications made on behalf of the plaintiff, but the INS decision does not contradict (federal law)."
However, the judges rejected a request by Elian's father to appoint him as Elian's representative in the ongoing trial in place of great uncle Lazaro González.
Making Juan Miguel González his son's legal representative would have allowed him to simply drop his asylum application and go home.
Elian's great uncle vowed to continue the fight, saying relatives in Miami could sue for visitation while the child was in the United States.
"We will continue to fight with the laws... so that Elian González can live in a free country as his mother wanted," Lazaro González said.
President Clinton and Attorney General Janet Reno praised the ruling, both stressing their confidence that Juan Miguel Gonzalez was the best person to raise his child.
& LR; < p > As I said, this case is about the importance of family and the bond between father and son, "Clinton said in a written statement from Berlin.
Elian's father asked his relatives in Miami to end their court battle after Thursday's ruling and allow him to finally return home with his son.
"A child should just be with his parents, always with his parents," the elder González said through an interpreter.
Speaking in English, he said: 'I want to thank the American people.
Elian was rescued by two American fishermen off the coast of Florida last November after his boat capsized.
Elian's mother and 10 others died, but the boy survived by clinging to a tube.
Elian's relatives in Miami took the child in but then refused to return it to his surviving father in Cuba, citing the harshness of the Castro regime.
Relatives in Miami applied for political asylum on Elian's behalf because the child did not want to return to Cuba.
INS agents traveled to Cuba to interview the child's father before the INS rejected the asylum application on the grounds that the child's father was the only person authorized to represent him.
A district judge upheld the federal authority.
Relatives in Miami appealed that decision and obtained an injunction requiring Elian to remain in the United States until the asylum issue found its way through the courts.
Juan Miguel Gonzalez arrived in the United States on April 2, and Reno ordered relatives in Miami to hand the child over to his father.
The family refused, and federal agents forcibly took the child during a raid on the family's Miami home on April 22.
The father and son lived on a large, private estate in Maryland for a month, but recently moved to an affluent neighborhood in Washington, D.C. to spend the rest of their time in the United States.
Shortly after the appellate panel issued its decision, Attorney Coffey made an emergency request to Supreme Court Justice Anthony M. Kennedy (who has jurisdiction over such matters from Florida), seeking assurances that Elian will remain in the United States until the Supreme Court can consider a formal appeal.
Reno, however, said the request to Kennedy had no purpose because the restraining order blocking Elian's return to Cuba still applies.
If the family does not appeal within 14 days, she said, Elian would be free to leave the country seven days later.
Justice Department officials, however, said the appeals court could decide to waive the additional seven-day deadline.
Even before the verdict was announced, protesters gathered outside the relatives' home in Miami.
They cried and cried when the decision was announced.
In a statement read out on Cuban television, Castro's government expressed displeasure at a further delay in Elian's return.
The government called the 14-day wait "another concession to the mafia" - a disparaging term the government often uses to describe Miami's exiled Cubans.
Jose Basulto, founder of Brothers to the Rescue, a Cuban exile group that often rescues Cuban refugees from the Florida Strait, called the latest ruling "outrageous."
"The story of Elian Gonzalez is a turning point in relations between the Cuban community and the United States," Basulto said.
'This day will be remembered in the history of Cuba's relationship with the United States as much as the Bay of Pigs. '
Cubans in exile failed to overthrow Castro in 1961, when the communist leader repelled their invasion of the Bay of Pigs.
South Africa on Friday deplored the morning bombings outside the United States embassies in Kenya and Tanzania that killed dozens and injured more than 1,000 others.
A State Department statement called the attacks senseless.
"The South African government deplores these senseless acts against the American embassies in Nairobi and Dar es Salaam and would like to express its condolences to the victims of the explosions," the statement said.
A massive explosion reportedly damaged the U.S. Embassy in Nairobi, killing 40 people and injuring at least 1,000.
Kenyan Commerce Minister Joseph Kamotho and U.S. Ambassador to Kenya Prudence Bushnell were injured in the blast.
Also on Friday morning, at least four people were killed in an explosion near the American Embassy in Dar es Salaam, the capital of Tanzania.
As far as can be determined, no South Africans were affected by the explosions, the South African Foreign Ministry said.
The flu season is drawing to a close and has so far claimed the lives of 105 children - about as many as the average.
The season began about a month earlier than usual, stoking fears it could turn into the worst in a decade.
In the end, it was very hard for the elderly, but overall it was moderately hard, according to the Centers for Disease Control and Prevention.
Six of the child deaths have been reported in the past week, and it's possible there will be more, Dr. Michael Jhung of the CDC said Friday.
In an average flu season, about 100 children die.
The exception was the swine flu pandemic of 2009-2010, when 348 children died.
The CDC recommends that all children 6 months and older be vaccinated against flu every season, even though only about half get a flu shot or nasal spray.
All but four of the children who died were old enough to be vaccinated, but 90 percent of them were not vaccinated, CDC officials said.
This year's vaccine was considered effective in children, although it did not work particularly well in the elderly.
In this group, 177 out of every 100,000 people with flu-related illnesses have been hospitalized in recent months.
This flu season began in early December, a month earlier than usual, and peaked at the end of the year.
Since then, flu reports have been piling up across the country.
"We seem to be nearing the end of the flu season," Jhung said.
Barack Obama would make a great stand-up comic, not because he's the funniest president ever, but because he jokes the way many of us comedians do: as a weapon.
Traditionally, our presidents "(intentionally) funny lines had one thing in common: they were self-deprecating.
Sure, some presidents have used jokes to mock their opponents, but not to the extent that Obama has.
During his tenure, he has unleashed increasingly biting comedic barbs against his critics and political opponents.
These jokes are meant to do more than just entertain.
He is trying to undermine his opponents and get the crowd - in this case the public - on his side.
I can assure you that not only is it effective to control critics / hecklers, but it is also very satisfying when the crowd laughs at them.
A New York man accused of faking his death last summer pleaded guilty Thursday to a conspiracy charge, Nassau County District Attorney Kathleen Rice said.
Raymond Roth, 48, of Massapequa, New York, was first reported missing by his 22-year-old son Jonathan Roth in the waters off Jones Beach in late July last year.
Several days after an extensive search involving multiple agencies, authorities learned that the missing man was in South Carolina, where he had been stopped for speeding.
The day before Raymond Roth was pulled over, his wife Evana showed authorities emails she had discovered that appeared to reveal a plot between him and his son to fake his death.
Raymond Roth wanted his wife and son to collect at least $410,000 in life insurance benefits while he began a new life in Florida, Rice said.
State police arrested both men in early August on charges of insurance fraud, conspiracy and making false statements.
Raymond Roth agreed on Thursday to plead guilty in exchange for a 90-day prison sentence and five years of probation, prosecutors said.

Before the launch of Keep, which Google launched this week, there was no standard note-taking app for Android.
It was a glaring hole, considering that Apple's iPhone has built-in note and reminder apps that can run on Siri.
Rather than settle for a bare-bones app to fill the void, the search giant went one step further.
Still, it won't quite conquer the world.

That should scare Evernote.

The FAA announced Friday that it would close 149 control towers at regional airports because of forced spending cuts - and close 40 more that the FAA had been expected to close.
A four-week, phased closure of the 149 control towers will begin on April 7, the FAA said.
The FAA had been expected to announce the closure of 189 low- and medium-volume towers staffed by contractors.
Prior to Friday's announcement, it had said it would consider keeping a tower open if the airport convinced the authority that it was in the "national interest."
Under the Congressional mandate, the FAA must cut nearly $600 million of its nearly $48 billion budget this fiscal year.
Because the majority of the 47,000 employees are air traffic controllers, it is impossible to cut the budget without affecting air traffic controllers, the agency said.
President Barack Obama arrived in refugee-inundated Jordan on Friday after landing a diplomatic coup shortly before leaving Israel when Prime Minister Benjamin Netanyahu apologized to Turkey for a 2010 commando raid that killed nine militants on a Turkish ship in a Gaza flotilla.
The apology, long demanded by Turkish Prime Minister Recep Erdogan, has defused tense feelings between Turkey and Israel, two key US allies in the Middle East.
It happened in a phone call with Erdogan during a final meeting between Obama and Netanyahu at Tel Aviv's Ben Gurion International Airport a few minutes before Air Force One departed for Jordan to complete the president's Middle East trip.
A statement from Netanyahu's office said the apology included an offer of compensation.
There was confusion later, however, when the Israeli government dropped a reference to normalized relations between the countries with the return of ambassadors in its original statement.
There was also no word on whether the once-close nations would resume joint military exercises suspended after the flotilla attack.
Turkey had prosecuted four Israeli soldiers in absentia, and Israel initially said the two leaders had agreed to lift legal action against the troops.
However, this measure was later omitted in the amended declaration.
Microsoft Corp broke sales records in 2010 when it released its Kinect - a motion-tracking device that allowed Xbox users to play their favorite games through gestures alone without the need for a controller.
Now the software giant hopes to establish Kinect beyond gaming with the help of outside developers.
Microsoft is expanding into China with a company-funded incubator program for outside developers to build Kinect-based products for a range of industries, including healthcare and retail.
In the US, eleven companies were selected last year from nearly 500 applicants for the accelerator's first three-month course in Seattle.
Microsoft said it had identified three companies for the China program, which is scheduled to run through June.
The company gives each participating start-up in the Seattle program $20,000 to develop software that can be used with Kinect for Windows, but it does not ask for any ownership or intellectual property rights.
Microsoft also has no veto over the ideas.
What Microsoft is getting are developers around the world working on ideas that could potentially open Kinect technology for Windows to new markets - and convince consumers and businesses to buy the company's hardware and use the potential apps.
The Windows-enabled Kinect sensor, which would be necessary for any company wishing to take advantage of these potential new technologies, is currently being sold through Microsoft for $250, more than double what Microsoft charges for the Xbox-only version.
