Israeli Prime Minister Benjamin Netanyahu apologized Friday to Turkish Prime Minister Recep Tayyip Erdogan for the attack on a Turkish flotilla ship, a move that will help restore strained relations between the countries.
The call, which came as President Barack Obama wrapped up his first visit to Israel, was an unexpected result of a Middle East trip that seemed to yield few concrete steps.
Netanyahu's office confirmed that in a conversation with Erdogan, the Israeli leader "agreed to restore normalization between Israel and Turkey, including the sending of ambassadors and the cancellation of legal action against [Israeli Defense Forces] soldiers."
Erdogan accepted the Israeli apology, the prime minister's office said.
Erdogan has long sought an apology for the May 2010 raid on the Mavi Marmara, which was part of a flotilla trying to break Israel's blockade of Gaza.
Nine passengers, all of Turkish or Turkish descent, were killed in an Israeli attack on the ship.
"In light of the Israeli investigation into the incident, which highlighted several operational errors, Prime Minister Netanyahu apologized to the Turkish people for any mistakes that could have resulted in loss of life and agreed to the conclusion of the compensation agreement," the statement said.
Netanyahu told Erdogan that what happened aboard the Mavi Marmara was "unintentional and Israel expresses regret for the injuries and deaths," the statement said.
Obama later said at a news conference in Amman that he had spoken with both leaders over the past two years about how it was in the interest of both countries to restore normal relations.
He said he had discussed the issue with Netanyahu during his visit to Israel this week, and they agreed that the time was right for talks with the Turkish leader.
The incident was the subject of talks between Netanyahu and President Barack Obama during the president's trip to Israel this week, administration officials said.
Health insurers are warning private brokers that premiums for many individuals and small businesses could rise sharply next year as a result of the health care overhaul law, with the nation's largest company predicting that prices could more than double for some consumers who buy their own plans.
The forecasts, made in meetings with brokers and brokers, provide some of the most concrete evidence yet of how much insurers could raise prices when key provisions of the law come into effect next year - an issue that is hotly debated.
The projected increases are at odds with what consumers should expect overall in terms of costs, according to the Obama administration.
The Department of Health and Human Services says the law will "make health care more affordable and accessible," pointing to a 2009 Congressional Budget Office analysis that found average individual premiums would be lower on an apple-to-apple basis.
The gulf between some insurers "price talk and the government's projections suggests just how complicated the law's impact will be.
Airlines will submit price proposals to regulators in the coming months.
Part of the opacity stems from the role of government subsidies.
Federal subsidies under the health care law will help lower-income consumers cover costs, but they are not usually included in insurers' premium projections.
Many consumers will get more generous plans because of new legislation.
The impact of the law will vary widely, and insurers and other analysts agree that some consumers and small businesses are likely to see premiums fall.
Starting next year, the law will prevent insurers from selling coverage or setting premiums based on people's medical history, and reduce their ability to set rates based on age.
This can raise insurance prices for younger, healthier consumers and rein them in for older, sicker consumers.
The rules may also affect small businesses, which sometimes pay premiums linked to workers' health and history.
The last surviving member of the team that first conquered Everest in 1953 has died in a care home in Derbyshire.
George Lowe, 89, died in Ripley on Wednesday after a long illness, with his wife Mary by his side.
New Zealand-born Lowe was part of the team that helped Sir Edmund Hillary and Tenzing Norgay reach the summit in 1953.
Family friend and historian Dr Huw Lewis-Jones paid tribute to a "gentle soul and fine mountaineer" who shunned the limelight.
Lowe also participated in the 1957-58 Transantarctic Expedition, which made the first successful overland crossing of Antarctica over the South Pole.
Later he undertook expeditions to Greenland, Greece and Ethiopia.
Speaking to the BBC in 1995 about his Antarctic adventure, Lowe said: "We estimated we would make it in 100 days and we arrived on the 99th day.
It had a multiplier effect.
'We were glad England and New Zealand knew about it and we thought it'd stop. '
He also talked about his "second job" as the group's cameraman and how he had to wear four pairs of gloves to operate the clockwork camera.
Do you participate in the urgency - or do you absorb it? "he said.
Dr Lewis-Jones, the former curator at Cambridge University's Scott Polar Research Institute who first met Lowe in 2005, called him a "hero."
"I don't use that word often, but it's not often you meet one," he said.
A book of memoirs and photos of Lowe's rise, which he worked on with Dr Lewis-Jones, is due to be published in May.
He said: "Lowe was a brilliant, kind guy who never sought the limelight... and 60 years after Everest his achievements deserve wider recognition.
"He was involved in two of the most important explorations of the 20th century... yet he remained a humble, happy man to the end... an inspiring lesson for all of us."
Before retiring in 1984, Mr Lowe worked as a schools inspector in the Department of Education and Science and is survived by three sons from a previous marriage.
The last British climber on the 1953 team, Mike Westmacott, died last June.

Oil giant BP has announced it will buy back $8 billion worth of shares and return to shareholders the money it put into a complicated Russian venture.
It said it "expects to return to BP shareholders an amount equal to the value of the company's original investment in TNK-BP."
BP agreed in October to return its 50% stake in TNK-BP to Russia's Rosneft for $17.1 billion in cash and shares.
It invested in TNK-BP in 2003.
BP chief executive Carl-Henric Svanberg said: "We expect our stake in Rosneft to create long-term value for BP and its shareholders.
But this buyback program should also allow our shareholders to benefit in the short term from the value we have realized through the restructuring of our Russia business. "
BP expects the share buyback program to last between 12 and 18 months.
"In 2003, BP invested approximately $8 billion in cash, shares and assets in the creation of TNK-BP," the oil giant said.
"Over the next decade, BP received a total of $19 billion in dividends from the joint venture," he said.
In 2011, the AAR consortium tried to block a drilling joint venture between BP and Rosneft in the Arctic in court, and the plan was eventually dropped.
In addition to internal squabbles, BP employees at TNK-BP have fallen foul of Russian authorities.
During his time as boss of TNK-BP, Bob Dudley - now head of BP as a whole - had his office bugged and searched by police, encountering troublesome after-tax claims, legal action and visa problems.
He eventually fled the country in 2008.
But the tie-up with Rosneft will keep BP in Russia, allowing it to continue exploring and exploiting the country's vast energy resources, including in the Arctic.
Russian police and tax inspectors have raided offices of the human rights group Memorial and other foreign-funded civil society groups.
Memorial is famous for documenting human rights violations in Russia.
The U.S. Embassy in Moscow has expressed concern and asked the Russian government for an explanation.
A new Russian law requires foreign-funded non-governmental organizations (NGOs) associated with politics to register as "foreign agents" - a term that suggests espionage.
In the worst repression of the Soviet era, the label "foreign agents" was used to denounce dissidents - or simply political rivals of Joseph Stalin - which could lead to execution.
Memorial said inspectors returned to its Moscow offices on Friday after seizing 600 documents, including accounts, on Thursday.
A statement on Memorial's website said the inspections were directly related to the new law on NGOs and target groups' compliance with it.
Memorial director Arseny Roginsky was quoted by Russian news site Vesti as saying it was "a complete review of everything to do with our sources of funding."
He insisted that the NGO law "will not change our position at all."
"We will not refuse foreign donations or register as a" foreign agent, "" he said.
China's change of leadership this month took place against an ominous backdrop.
More than 16,000 dead pigs have been found in rivers supplying Shanghai with drinking water.
A haze resembling volcanic fumes enveloped the capital, causing a convulsive cough and obscuring the portrait of Mao Zedong at the gateway to the Forbidden City.
China's environmental problems, especially its polluting air, are so severe that senior officials have been forced to admit them openly.
Fu Ying, spokeswoman for the National People's Congress, said that every morning after opening her curtains, she checked for smog and kept face masks for her daughter and herself at home.
Li Keqiang, the new premier, said air pollution had left him "pretty upset" and vowed to "show even greater resolve and make more vigorous efforts" to eradicate the problem.
Last month, after deadly air pollution in northern China reached record levels, officials led by then-Premier Wen Jiabao unveiled tough new fuel standards that oil companies had blocked for years.
But there are doubts that oil companies will comply, especially since oil authorities opposed a similar government order for higher-grade fuel four years ago.
The companies routinely ignore government orders to modernize coal-fired power plants, according to the ministry.
And, as with oil companies, energy companies exert an outsized influence on environmental policy debates.
In 2011, during a panel discussion on stricter emissions standards, the China Electricity Council, which represents the companies, vehemently opposed the proposals, saying that the cost of modernizing the plants was too high.
On February 28, Deutsche Bank issued an analyst note saying that China's current economic policies would lead to a huge increase in coal consumption and car sales over the next decade.
"China's air pollution will worsen significantly from the already intolerable levels," the analysts said, calling for drastic policy changes and "a strong will on the part of the government to overcome opposition from interest groups."
The report projects that the number of passenger cars in China will rise to 400 million by 2030, up from 90 million today.
Should this happen, Cyprus's exit from the euro union would have a devastating impact on the country's citizens, who are among the most indebted in the eurozone.
The losses resulting from the collapse of Cypriot banks and the country's return to its former currency would be minimal compared to the chaos that Greece would have caused if it had not been bailed out.
And that, economists and investors argue, is why Germany and its Dutch stalking horse, Jeroen Dijsselbloem, the chairman of the Eurogroup of finance ministers, insisted so persistently that depositors - large and small, Cypriot and Russian - contribute €5.8 billion to bail out Cyprus's largest banks, amounting to €10 billion.
Greece may have been too big to fail last year, but Cyprus, which accounts for less than half a percent of the eurozone's gross domestic product, is certainly not.
Only Ireland, which is still struggling to recover from the banking collapse that required an international bailout in 2010, has a higher debt-to-GDP ratio among eurozone countries.
As debt in Europe rises in inverse proportion to the ability of its citizens, businesses, and governments to pay it, a view is emerging in Berlin and Brussels that a signal must be sent that citizens and investors must begin to accept losses for the eurozone's long-term survival.
"There have been too many bailouts in Europe; it's time to remove the airbags," said Stephen Jen, a former economist at the International Monetary Fund who runs a hedge fund in London.
"This is not Lehman," he said, alluding to the catastrophic chain reaction triggered by the collapse of Lehman Brothers in 2008.
Eric Dor is a French economist who has studied the mechanisms by which a country might withdraw from the monetary union.
Estimates of the potential cost of forcing Greece into a disorderly exit from the euro have varied between €200 billion and €800 billion, given the greater exposure of the European Central Bank and European banks to the country.
"This explains why Germany and others are putting so much pressure on Cyprus," said Dor, head of research at the Ieseg School of Management in Lille, France.
"They say that we can take the risk of pushing Cyprus out of the eurozone and that Europe can bear the losses without going bust."
Mr Dor points out that the current zone-wide system for protecting bank deposits up to €100,000 was introduced after the financial panic following the Lehman collapse.
These deposits will be insured by national governments.
When the President of Cyprus admitted this week that his country did not have the money to secure €30 billion in guaranteed bank deposits - a figure larger than the Cypriot economy itself - a crucial bond of trust between a government and its citizens was torn.
"It is the first time ever that the leader of a eurozone country has admitted that he could not afford to pay the guarantee," Dor said.
A hasty exit from the eurozone would make Cypriot people's savings all the more volatile once they are converted back into Cypriot pounds, the currency that Cyprus used before the introduction of the euro in 2007.

An hour before her colleagues gathered for the first vote of a new Congress, Sen. Kelly Ayotte slipped into an empty Senate chamber to enjoy the splendor of her legislative home.
As Ms. Ayotte, an aspiring Republican from New Hampshire, sat down at the wooden desk where generations of lawmakers from her state had cast their ballots, a bouncer marched purposefully toward her.
The desks, he told her sternly, were for senators only.
On that January day in 2011, Ayotte was inducted into the rarest ranks of the country's political class, namely female senators.
"The desk thing really stuck with me," Ayotte said.
In the 90 years since Rebecca Felton of Georgia became the first woman in the US Senate - sworn in just 24 hours - women remain an anomaly in the House of Lords.
But now that 20 female senators are in office, an all-time high, women have gone from the curiosity they exercised for much of the twentieth century to an important new force in key committees and legislation.
For the first time, a woman - Senator Barbara A. Mikulski, a Democrat from Maryland - heads the Senate Budget Committee, which distributes billions of dollars annually to the government and has long been dominated by men.
Senator Patty Murray, a Democrat from Washington, is the first chairwoman of the Budget Committee and tasked with shaping Democratic strategy in the budget fight on Capitol Hill.
One of the biggest bills that passed the Senate last year was farm legislation led by Senator Debbie Stabenow, a Michigan Democrat who chairs the Agriculture Committee.
Senator Barbara Boxer, the California Democrat who chairs the Environment and Public Works Committee, has pushed the highway bill.
"We're getting more and more numerous," said Senator Amy Klobuchar, Democrat of Minnesota.
'But more importantly, we're growing in power.
When I started only six years ago, it was unusual for a woman to manage a bill. "
Equally important, even male senators say, is the potential for women to change the tenor of the Senate and push for compromise in the highly partisan atmosphere on Capitol Hill.
"I don't want to generalize because that doesn't apply to everyone, but they tend to find common ground," said Senator Rob Portman, Republican of Ohio.
'So I think it's going to have a positive impact on the Senate. '
Though partisan division is the central feature of the modern Congress, women have begun to break out of gridlock by forming coalitions that have taken leaders of both parties by surprise.
Senator Kirsten E. Gillibrand, Democrat of New York, and Senator Susan Collins, Republican of Maine, led the Senate repeal of the "don't ask, don't tell" law in 2010, which allowed gay men and women to serve openly in the military.
This year, all four Senate Republicans split from their party and voted with Senate Democrats to reauthorize the Violence Against Women Act, which expired during the last Congress.

JERUSALEM (AP) - A friend of Yitzhak Rabin's assassin spoke out Friday in her own defense, saying she regretted calling the prime minister a traitor and praying for his death.
Margalit Har-Shefi, 22, has pleaded not guilty to failing to report Yigal Amir's plan to kill Rabin.
She stood in a Tel Aviv court for more than four hours on Friday.
Amir, 27, is serving a life sentence for killing Rabin at a peace rally in Tel Aviv in November 1995.
According to newspaper reports, Amir was in love with Har-Shefi and may have tried to impress her by killing the prime minister.
Har-Shefi admitted that she told police interrogators that Rabin was a traitor and that she prayed that he would suffer a heart attack and die.
She said Rabin's assassination was "a black mark on Israel," Israeli radio and army radio reported.
Har-Shefi said she heard Amir talk about killing Rabin but did not inform the police because she did not believe he was serious.
Instead, she turned to her community rabbi in the Jewish settlement of Beit El in the West Bank and told him about Amir's statements.
The rabbi called talk of Rabin's killing "nonsense" and said Jews should not kill each other, the radio reports said.
Har-Shefi described Amir as an "original thinker," "delusional and a liar."
She also rejected accusations by Amir's brother Hagai that she had joined an anti-Arab underground movement.
She said she gave the Amir brothers false information about the location of a cache of weapons and ammunition when they asked her to help organize such a movement.

BUDAPEST, Hungary (AP) _ Hungarian astronaut Bertalan Farkas is tired of being marginalized and going to the United States to start a new career, he said Saturday.
"At 48, it's too early to retire," a fit Farkas said on state television's morning talk show.
Together with the American astronaut Jon McBride, Farkas founded an American-Hungarian joint venture called Orion in 1980, which manufactures technologies for space travel.
Farkas will move to the company's U.S. headquarters.
Farkas, an Air Force captain, was sent into space aboard Soyuz 36 on May 26, 1980.
He spent six days aboard the Salyut 6 spacecraft with the three Soviet astronauts Valeri Kubasov, Leonid Popov and Valeri Ryumin.
McBride, 54, of Lewisburg, West Virginia, was part of a seven-person crew aboard the orbiter Challenger in October 1984 and later served as NASA's deputy administrator for congressional relations.
Farkas said he hopes to one day follow in the footsteps of fellow astronaut John Glenn, who at 77 is on the verge of returning to space.
On 22 May 1995, Farkas was appointed Brigadier General and, the following year, Military Attaché at the Hungarian Embassy in Washington.
However, Farkas, who was charged by the District of Columbia traffic police in December with driving under the influence of alcohol, was ordered home and retired.
ISTANBUL, Turkey (AP) _ About 1,500 ethnic Albanians marched in downtown Istanbul on Sunday, burning Serbian flags to protest the killing of ethnic Albanians by Serb police in the southern Serbian province of Kosovo.
Police prevented the crowd from reaching the Yugoslav consulate in downtown Istanbul, but allowed them to demonstrate on nearby streets.
"Stop the bloodshed in Kosovo," protesters chanted.
In the capital Ankara, meanwhile, a few hundred ethnic Albanians laid a black wreath at the gate of the Yugoslav Embassy.
The Serbian police say they are trying to eliminate the pro-independence Kosovo Liberation Army and restore order.
Clashes between Serb police and ethnic Albanians in the troubled region have killed at least 51 people in recent days.
Concerned that the civil war in Kosovo could spread to the entire Balkans, Turkey called on all Balkan countries to sign a joint declaration to resolve the Kosovo crisis peacefully and without border changes.
MOSCOW (AP) _ Presidents Leonid Kuchma of Ukraine and Boris Yeltsin of Russia signed an economic cooperation plan Friday, and Yeltsin claimed they had solved more nagging problems.
Russia and Ukraine share similar cultures and languages, and Ukraine has been ruled from Moscow for centuries.
But, while the two Slavic neighbors see themselves as natural partners, their relations have been strained by a series of disputes since the Soviet Union's disintegration: Black Sea naval bases, border problems, and Ukraine's natural gas debt.
"We have covered the entire list of issues and discussed how we will address them," Yeltsin was quoted as saying by the ITAR-Tass news agency.
< unk > I have to say that there are no more unsolved problems.
We've solved them all. ""
But his full statement gave no details and gave no indication of how the disputes might be resolved.
Their solution would require a compromise between the parliaments of the two nations.
A major point of contention is a comprehensive political treaty on demarcation, which the two presidents signed last May.
Indeed, the treaty amounts to Russia's recognition of Ukraine's sovereignty and borders, and the Ukrainian parliament has already ratified it.
But Russia has blocked ratification, trying to tie it to an agreement that would allow the Russian navy to use a naval base on Ukraine's Crimean peninsula for at least another 20 years.
In their joint statement issued after the talks, Yeltsin and Kuchma called for ratification of the treaty as soon as possible, which would create a "strong legal basis" for bilateral relations and contribute to stability in Europe.
Kuchma had assured Yeltsin that Ukraine would not join NATO, Yeltsin's spokesman Sergei Yastrzhembski said, according to the Interfax news agency.
Russia's leaders have staunchly opposed the Western alliance's expansion into Eastern Europe.
In their joint statement issued by the Kremlin, the two Heads of State also undertook to consult regularly on their approaches to relations with NATO, and in particular on their actions under NATO's Partnership for Peace programme.
Last year, Russian officials attacked Ukraine for holding joint naval exercises with NATO in the Black Sea - an area Moscow considers its own territory.
Kuchma has repeatedly said that Ukraine will remain neutral for the foreseeable future.
Yeltsin and Kuchma also called for the development of stagnant relations between members of the Commonwealth of Independent States, a loose coalition of former Soviet republics.
In the past, Russia has often claimed that Ukraine is undermining efforts for closer cooperation within the CIS.
A key goal of Kuchma's four-day state visit was to sign a 10-year economic program aimed at doubling the two nations "trade turnover, which fell to $14 billion last year from $2.5 billion in 1996.
The two presidents signed the plan on Friday, which provides for cooperation in the fields of metallurgy, fuel, energy, aircraft construction, rocket, space and chemical industries.
A major project is the joint production of An-70 cargo planes, the Kremlin statement said.
The program also calls for coordination of economic reforms and joint improvement of social programs in the two countries, where many people have become impoverished during the chaotic post-Soviet transition to capitalism.
Kuchma also wanted to visit Russian gas giant Gazprom, likely to discuss Ukraine's $1.2 billion debt to the company.
ADDIS ABABA, Ethiopia (AP) _ The Organization of African Unity said Friday it will investigate the Hutu-organized genocide of more than 500,000 Tutsis in Rwanda nearly four years ago.
The foreign ministers of the member states meeting in the Ethiopian capital agreed to set up a seven-member panel to investigate who shot down the plane of Rwandan President Juvenal Habyarimana on 6 April 1994.
The assassination triggered a murderous rampage by Hutu security forces and civilians, who slaughtered mainly Tutsis, but also Hutus who advocated reconciliation with the minority.
It also rekindled the civil war.
The panel will also look at the exodus of some 2 million Rwandan Hutus to neighbouring countries where they lived in UN refugee camps that they ran for two and a half years.
The investigation will examine the role of internal and external forces before and after the genocide, as well as the role of the United Nations and its agencies and the OAU before, during and after the genocide, "the OAU said.
The panel will be based in Addis Ababa and complete its investigation within a year, it said.
It will be financed by voluntary contributions from Germany and abroad.

Former Savings Bank boss Charles Keating is facing further legal problems in California.
A federal appeals court has overturned his conviction for securities fraud.
Ninety-nine years ago, Charles Keating was convicted in state court of aiding and abetting the fraud of thousands of investors who bought high-risk junk bonds sold by Keating's associates at Lincoln Savings and Loan.

Nineteen of Keating's convictions were rejected on formal grounds.
And on that basis, Keating was released from prison before he was eligible for parole.

That means the convictions stand, a ruling that Keating's lawyers are likely to send back to the state court, where they must begin a new appeal process.
NEWARK, N.J. _ A new Essex County task force began investigating the killings of 14 black women in the Newark area over the past five years on Thursday, as law enforcement acknowledged they had to work harder to solve the cases of murdered women.
Police and prosecutors said they had identified several suspects in six of the cases and had not yet found a link between the killings or the victims, several of whom are believed to have been prostitutes.
State, regional and local law enforcement officials have expressed concern in recent months about a possible pattern of murdered women and a disproportionate number of unsolved cases.
Citing one example, Sgt. Derek Glenn, a spokesman for the Newark Police Department, said that of nine women killed last year, only four had suspects arrested.
Overall, however, there have been arrests in more than 60 percent of homicides.
Eight of the 14 murders since 1993 have already been investigated by the Newark Police Department, Glenn said.
Of the eight victims, three were stabbed, two strangled, two beaten to death and one suffocated, he said, and these different killing methods and other evidence seem to indicate that the eight cases are unrelated.
But with the task force's investigation only just under way, officials have been careful not to draw firm conclusions, leaving open the possibility of a serial killer in some cases.
"I haven't seen a pattern," said Patricia Hurt, the Essex County prosecutor who launched the task force on Tuesday.
"These women's lifestyles are extremely dangerous," she said.

Libya, which took the case to the United Nations' highest judicial body in a dispute with the United States and Britain, welcomed the ruling and said it would press again for a trial in a third neutral country.

WASHINGTON _ The economy created surprisingly robust jobs in January, the government said Friday, evidence that America's economic staying power has so far withstood all the disruption caused by the financial turmoil in Asia.
The Bureau of Labor Statistics said the economy added 358,000 jobs last month, far more than the 235,000 forecast by economists.
With growing job opportunities luring more people into the labor market, the unemployment rate remained at 4.7% last month, just above its lowest level in a quarter-century.
Demand for labor also led employers to raise wages again last month.
Average hourly earnings for manufacturing and non-supervisory workers rose 4 cents to $12.51.
The increase pushed wages 3.8% higher than a year earlier, continuing a trend that gave workers back some of the earning power they had lost to inflation over the past decade.
Economists, however, said the wage increase was not enough to raise concerns about higher inflation.
And, because the Federal Reserve has made it clear that it expects the economy to slow in the coming months as ripple effects from Asia reach the United States, investors ignored the big job gains and bet that neither a rise in interest rates nor resurgent inflation is imminent.
Stocks rose, pushing the Dow Jones Industrial Average up 72.24 points to 8,189.49, leaving the index within 70 points of its August 6 record high.
In the bond market, a sensitive barometer of inflation fears, prices rose, pushing the yield on the 30-year Treasury note to 5.92 percent from 5.93 percent.
"Job creation is robust and we have effective price stability," said Paul McCulley, an economist at UBS Securities.
With potential inflation offset by worries about possible price declines, the Fed and its chairman, Alan Greenspan, have indicated that they intend to leave interest rates unchanged until the economy clearly tilts one way or the other.
January's employment figures extended a surge in job gains that began last fall when the labor market showed signs of cooling.
After a slight downward revision from Friday to December, the economy added 358,000 jobs in the last four months and 381,000 in the last three months, up from an average of 242,000 in the first nine months of 1997.
The increase in jobs reflects a remarkable convergence of positive and self-reinforcing economic forces.
After a brief, steep slump when Asia's financial problems first became apparent, the stock market has recovered and corporate profits have remained healthy.
Long-term interest rates, an important indicator of economic stability, have fallen in recent months.
Lower interest rates have helped boost housing and other interest-sensitive sectors of the economy by making credit more affordable and allowing businesses to continue to invest heavily in more efficient equipment.
The likelihood that the federal budget will soon move from deficit to surplus has further improved the outlook.
President Clinton welcomed the figures at a news conference on Friday.
His advisers said the results reflected not only the balanced budget, but also initiatives such as improved access to education and training and opening foreign markets to trade.
The biggest job gains were in construction, which added 92,000 jobs in January.
The rise reflected the strength of the housing sector, which was boosted not only by falling interest rates but also by unusually warm weather.
The services sector also posted solid job gains, as did manufacturers - two areas likely to be hardest hit when the effects of the Asian crisis hit the American economy.
The biggest damage from Asia is likely to come from the fall in the value of many Asian currencies against the dollar - a situation that is expected to lead to an increase in cheap imports to the United States, hurting American competition.
And the weakness of Asian economies is also likely to lead to a decline in demand for American exports.
"The economy is running on the fumes of strength from before the Asian shock," McCulley said.
"These fumes will run out and the manufacturing sector will start to beat itself up in the spring." "
The adult male unemployment rate fell to 3.8 percent last month from 4.1 percent in December, the lowest level in nearly two decades, according to the data.
For adult women, the rate rose 0.4 percent to 4.4 percent.
For the first time, the Labor Department broke down unemployment by educational level, gender, age and race.
The analysis showed that unemployment rates fell steadily with higher levels of education.
O'SMACH, Cambodia (AP) _ The top commander of a Cambodian resistance force said Thursday he has sent a team to recover the remains of a British mine-clearing expert kidnapped and presumed killed by Khmer Rouge guerrillas nearly two years ago.
Gen. Nhek Bunchhay, a loyalist of ousted Cambodian Prime Minister Prince Norodom Ranariddh, said in an interview with The Associated Press at his hilltop headquarters that he hoped to recover the remains of Christopher Howes within the next two weeks.
Howes had been working for the UK-based Mines Advisory Group when he was kidnapped with his Cambodian interpreter Houn Hourth in March 1996.
Howes' team was 17 kilometres from Angkor Wat, the fabled 11th century temple that is Cambodia's main tourist attraction, when it was attacked.
In January this year, British police searching for Howes concluded that he had probably been killed shortly after his capture.
The Foreign Office said it had informed the family of Howes, who was 37 at the time of his abduction, that he was likely to have died within weeks or months of his capture on 26 March 1996.

Thai military officials who oversee Cambodian affairs said privately on Thursday that Britain, through its embassies in Thailand and Cambodia, had gone to great lengths to resolve Howe's case on the eve of the second anniversary of his abduction.
Nhek Bunchhay, who was closely involved in the search for Howes before he was forced to flee the Cambodian capital following a coup last year, said he was confident Howes' remains would be found.
He said he had received information from Khmer Rouge guerrillas about where the body was buried, and recently ordered 10 of his men from a 500-strong force near Khmer Rouge headquarters in Anlong Veng to conduct the search.
If and when the remains were found, he said, they would be handed over to the British Embassy, apparently meaning they would be sent across the border to Thailand and on to Bangkok.
Nhek Bunchhay said he now believes Howes was killed within a week of his capture by a Khmer Rouge group loyal to Pol Pot, the guerrilla group's leader at the time.
Pol Pot is blamed for the radical policies that led to the deaths of 1.7 million Cambodians when the communist group was in power in the late 1970s.
When Howes was captured, the Khmer Rouge were a more or less unified guerrilla force with more than 10,000 men under arms.
But the group began to break up in mid-1996 after one of its leaders, Ieng Sary, defected.
Other commanders and thousands of guerrillas followed him.
A small hardcore group led by Pol Pot remained at their headquarters in Anlong Veng in northern Cambodia, near the Thai border.
But in June last year there was a rift between the remaining leadership and Pol Pot was arrested.
Last July, in Cambodia's capital Phnom Penh, Second Prime Minister Hun Sen staged a successful coup against First Prime Minister Prince Norodom Ranariddh.
Ranariddh's loyalists, including his top military commander Nhek Bunchhay, went into hiding or fled the capital.
Nhek Bunchhay evaded an intense manhunt and made his way to O'Smach, the last major outpost of Ranariddh's forces.
They were supported by Khmer Rouge guerrillas from Anlong Veng, who formed an alliance with Ranariddh's resistance forces against Hun Sen.
CANBERRA, Australia (AP) _ Qantas will almost double its flights between Australia and India by August in search of new markets unaffected by the crippling Asian financial crisis.
The move comes barely a month after Qantas suspended a number of services between Australia, Indonesia, Thailand and Malaysia in the wake of the Asian economic crisis.
The airline has also cancelled all flights to South Korea.
Qantas is planning daily flights between Sydney and Bombay from its current four flights a week to boost business and tourism ties with India, the airline said on Friday.
A joint statement with Tourism Minister Andrew Thomson said two new flights would leave Bombay on Mondays and Tuesdays from 30 March and the third every Thursday from 6 August.
This will add nearly 700 seats per week on the route.
Thomson, who spoke to leading tourism figures in India, said the flights would provide additional support to the growing tourism market.
Qantas India manager Khursheed Lam said the airline was working closely with the Australian Tourist Commission to develop greater awareness of Australia in the Indian market.
Qantas will also appoint a Bombay-based PR consultant.

CANBERRA, Australia (AP) _ A constitutional convention voted overwhelmingly Friday to make Australia a republic under its own president.
Prime Minister John Howard, himself a monarchist, promised to put the issue to a national referendum next year after delegates to Congress voted 89-52 in favor of a republic with 11 abstentions.
Spontaneous applause echoed through the hall and the public gallery as the crucial vote went through by a wide margin.
"I want a referendum," Howard said.
'The Australian people deserve the opportunity to have their say, 'he said.
"It would be a travesty of the common sense of Australian democracy if this claim were not put to the Australian people," Mr Howard said.
Even in his own cabinet, Howard is becoming increasingly isolated with his monarchist stance.
Treasurer Peter Costello, Environment Minister Robert Hill and Justice Minister Daryl Williams voted for the republic on Friday.
"This convention will be seen as a turning point in our history," Australian Republican Movement spokeswoman Mary Delahunty said.
Pro-republicans hope to have an Australian president by the opening of the 2000 Olympics and the 100th anniversary of the founding of the Australian Federation.
Republican Janet Holmes a Court called for a constitutional amendment with a new preamble to be written by Australian poets: "We need the smell of eucalyptus in it and the feeling of red dust."
The monarchists hope to defeat the republic in the referendum.
"The phoney war is over and the real referendum campaign has clearly begun," said Kerry Jones, the executive director of Australians for a Constitutional Monarchy.
"Now we may say," God save the Queen, for nothing will save the republic, "" indignant monarchist delegate David Mitchell.
"The idea of a republic will crumble to dust by the grace of Almighty God." "
Polls show public support for a republic rising, from about 35 percent a few years ago to about 51 percent this year, as pro-monarchist sentiment wanes.
Australia has been independent since 1901 but, like many Commonwealth countries, still recognises the British monarch as head of state.
Howard said Queen Elizabeth II was very interested in the conference.
I can assure you that she is following very closely what I have been told and believes that it is up to us.
'It's no secret that she's signalled to me... she's a 20th century democrat, understands that the role of the Crown in Australia is in the hands of the Australian people, as it has been for almost a century. '
The model for a republic, adopted by supporters of the direct election of a president over bitter objections, requires presidential nominations to be made with public participation and the winning candidate to be determined by a two-thirds majority in parliament.
Former prime minister Paul Keating, who thrust the issue of republic into the spotlight in his unsuccessful 1996 re-election campaign, welcomed the result.

The Persian Gulf showdown between Iraq and the United States took a more personal turn on Thursday when Iraqi President Saddam Hussein called President Bush a liar and said the outbreak of holy war could bring thousands of Americans home in coffins.
Commenting on the two-week Gulf crisis from his vacation home in Maine, Bush said he saw little reason to be optimistic about resolving the dispute stemming from Iraq's invasion of oil-rich Kuwait and its subsequent military buildup on the border with Saudi Arabia.
After a two-hour meeting at his Kennebunkport home with King Hussein of Jordan, Bush said, "I have no sense of hope" that Iraq would withdraw its army from Kuwait.
Bush also said Thursday that King Hussein had assured him that Jordan would close the last remaining free port for most of Iraq's trade as the economic embargo on materials to Iraq continues unabated.
Food is one of the goods denied entry; Iraq imports about three-quarters of its food.
Pentagon sources in Washington, meanwhile, said the Bush administration plans to send 45,000 Marines to the region to support the thousands of troops already in the Gulf and the Saudi desert.
At a news conference, Secretary of State James A. Baker III said Jordan was seeking "guidance" on a provision in the UN-backed trade embargo that allows food for humanitarian purposes.
Concern grew, however, for the safety of Americans and other Westerners stranded in Kuwait.
Iraqi military authorities had ordered all Americans and Britons in Kuwait to gather at a hotel, officials said.
"Very few" of the 2,500 Americans in occupied Kuwait complied with the order, a senior U.S. official told the Associated Press news agency.
Iraq said the raid was intended to protect them from unspecified threats; British Foreign Secretary William Waldegrave called the order "serious and sinister."
"What we fear is that they will be interned somewhere, most likely in Iraq," Waldegrave said.
In addition to the estimated 45,000 Marines who will eventually be part of Operation Desert Shield, stealth aircraft and the aircraft carrier John F. Kennedy will fly to Saudi Arabia to protect the country from Iraqi expansionism.
Pentagon spokesman Pete Williams said in Washington that Iraq has continued to increase its forces in Kuwait and now has about 160,000 troops.
Saddam has been under international quarantine since coming to power on August 2 in what he calls an "eternal merger" with Kuwait.
In a lengthy verbal attack read out on Iraqi television on Thursday, Saddam repeatedly called Bush a "liar" and said a rampage could produce body bags for Baghdad.
"We continue to pray and pray hard to God that there will be no confrontation in which you receive thousands of Americans wrapped in sad coffins after pushing them into a dark tunnel," Saddam said.
He described the US soldiers stationed in Saudi Arabia as the real occupiers in the Persian Gulf.
State Department Deputy Spokesman Richard Boucher responded, "We haven't really analyzed the statement in detail, but it seems to be just another example of his outlandish rhetoric and attempts to distort the truth.
"We believe that his words cannot distract the world from the facts of Iraqi aggression."
Since the Iraqi invasion, triggered by disputes over oil, land, and the repayment of war credits, an international land, sea, and air force has mobilized.
In the largest US military operation since Vietnam, an estimated 20,000 American GIs have already gathered to defend Saudi Arabia.
"We don't just arrive," said four-star Gen. John Dailey, deputy commander of the U.S. Marine Corps.
"We're here to stay for a pretty long period of time." "
Egypt, Syria, Morocco, and Bangladesh also sent ground troops, but on a much smaller scale.
The U.S. Navy has 27 ships stationed in Iraq's naval blockade.
They are supported by Great Britain, West Germany, Australia, Canada, the Netherlands and Belgium.
Bush was expected to authorize naval commanders to use "the minimum force necessary" to ban supplies to and from Iraq, a U.S. official said.
This could include shooting across the bow to bring a ship to a standstill.
In the air, US Air Force pilots say they are engaged in a "cat-and-mouse game" with Iraqi warplanes, which withdrew when the weapons radar was aimed at them.
"They don't want to play with us," said a U.S. crew chief.
In Kuwait, the Iraqis have covered the capital with an air defense system, according to a U.S. official who wished to remain anonymous.
He declined to say whether the weapons included missiles, but the Iraqis have them in their arsenal.
The combined operations aim to isolate and strangle Iraq until it withdraws from Kuwait.
The quarantine is intended to stop the flow of Iraqi oil, which is Iraq's economic lifeblood, and to prevent access to food and supplies.
With the conquest of Kuwait, Iraq now controls 20 percent of the world's oil reserves.
Only Saudi Arabia has more oil reserves.
Lloyd's List International, which monitors global shipping, said the Iraqi fleet of 80 tankers and cargo ships had ceased regular trading.
John Prescott, a shipping correspondent, said there was no shipping in Kuwaiti or Iraqi ports and that activity in the Jordanian port of Aqaba was slowing.
Bush's main goal in his meeting with Hussein was to urge the king to close Iraq's food and oil supply route from Aqaba on the Red Sea.
Now that the international noose has tightened, Aqaba is the only outlet in Iraq.
Bush has indicated that the U.S. Navy will barricade the port from Iraqi ships.
The president has also offered to compensate Jordan, because 40 percent of its exports go to Iraq and 90 percent of its oil comes from there.
"It is our only access to the sea and the rest of the world," Hussein said.
On trade with Iraq, he said: "There are no deliveries at the moment."
A day earlier, dozens of trucks, many with Iraqi license plates, streamed from Aqaba to Amman and onto the desert highway toward Iraq.
The Jordanian monarch met with Saddam this week, but he told reporters he had no message from Baghdad.
"I'm not speaking on behalf of anyone in the region... except myself," Hussein said.
At the United Nations, Libya called for US troops in the Persian Gulf to be replaced by Arab League troops and UN soldiers.
Libyan leader Muammar el-Qaddafi, in a letter to the UN Secretary-General, also called for an emergency Security Council meeting in Geneva to withdraw US troops.
Thirty-two of the 159 U.N. members had submitted compliance reports by Wednesday, and all complied with the sanctions.
